Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
6 min
Reductio ad absurdum on ignoring clear texts
Nasafi refutes concealment claim via reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses a reductio ad absurdum argument to refute the Rafidi claim that a clear text (nass) was concealed, and why this argument is logically forceful.

Core Idea
If ignoring a clear text is allowed, then the entire religion can be overturned.

Nasafi confronts a dangerous claim: that a clear text designating 'Ali as caliph existed but was suppressed. To show its absurdity, he builds a powerful analogy. If such suppression were permissible, then anyone could claim that the core pillars of Islam were abrogated and that the Companions simply ignored them. This would unravel the entire religion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation slowly, noting the rhetorical question that sets up the analogy.
The author begins with 'Then it is said to them' – he is addressing the opponents directly.
2
Identify the analogy
Notice how Nasafi compares the claimed suppression of a nass to a hypothetical abrogation of the five pillars: prayer, Hajj, Zakat, and fasting.
The analogy is designed to show that the same reasoning would lead to an absurd outcome.
3
Analyze the dilemma
See how he forces the opponent into either admitting a distinction (if they say no) or accepting the absurd conclusion (if they say yes), which nullifies the religion.
The dilemma is a classic reductio: both horns lead to contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم: لو جاز الإعراض عن مثل هذا النص الوارد في مثل هذه الحادثة التي تعم بها البلوى، فما تنكرون على من زعم أن الله تعالى نسخ هذه الصلوات والحج والزكاة وصوم شهر رمضان وفرض مكانها عبادات أُخر إلا أن الصحابة أعرضوا عن قبولها وأجمعوا على ترك العمل بها أفيصح قول هذا الزعم؟ فإن قالوا: لا - طولبوا بالفرق بين هذا وبين ما يدعون. وإن قالوا: نعم يصح هذا فقد أبطلوا الدين ورفضوا الشرائع، ولم يبق دليل أن هذه الشريعة شريعة الرسول عليه السلام وأن هذا الدين دينه، وهو أقوى طرق الإلحاد، وأوضح سبل هذا العناد - عصمنا الله تعالى عن ذلك ولقد صدق من قال: أروّض رافضيا لي صغيرا - أريكم منه زنديقا كبيرا
Then it is said to them: If it is permissible to disregard such a clear text concerning such a widespread event, then what do you deny regarding one who claims that Allah abrogated these prayers, Hajj, Zakat, and fasting of Ramadan and imposed other worships in their place, except that the Companions turned away from accepting them and unanimously agreed to abandon acting upon them? Is the claim of this one valid? If they say: No, they are required to differentiate between this and what they claim. And if they say: Yes, this is valid, then they have nullified the religion and discarded the laws, and no proof remains that this Shari'ah is the Shari'ah of the Messenger, peace be upon him, and that this religion is his religion, and this is the strongest path of atheism and the clearest way of this obstinacy. May Allah protect us from that. Indeed, truthful is he who said: I train a young Rafidi, I show you a great heretic from him.
Source pages p. 1129

Commentary
This passage is a reductio ad absurdum argument. The author addresses the Rafidis who claim that a clear text (nass) designated 'Ali as caliph but was concealed due to enmity. He argues that if it were permissible to ignore such a widespread clear text regarding a critical event, then it would be equally valid for someone to claim that the fundamental pillars of Islam (prayer, Hajj, Zakat, fasting) were abrogated, with the same excuse that the Companions ignored them. The author presents a dilemma: either the opponents reject this analogy, in which case they must explain the difference, or they accept it, which would nullify the entire religion. The passage concludes with a poetic condemnation of the Rafidi position.

Key Terms
نص
إعراض
نسخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the author is rejecting the possibility of nass entirely; rather, he is refuting the claim that nass existed but was suppressed.
Correction: The argument does not deny that a clear text could exist; it shows the logical contradiction in claiming that such a text was ignored by the Companions while still accepting the authenticity of the Shari'ah.

Checkpoint
What does the author use as an analogy to show the absurdity of the Rafidi claim that a clear text was ignored?
A) Abrogation of the five daily prayers, Hajj, Zakat, and fasting
B) Denial of prophethood
C) Rejection of the Quran
D) Abandoning the sunnah

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's argument shows that allowing suppression of a clear text would logically lead to the nullification of the entire Shariah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a clear command from Allah because it seemed too difficult or because you found a 'reason' to ignore it? How does this argument expose the danger of such selective obedience? What one command are you most tempted to disregard, and what would it cost your iman to admit that you have no valid excuse?
?
If you truly believed that ignoring a single clear text could unravel the entire religion, would you treat every revealed ruling with the same urgency? Where in your life have you treated some commands as less binding? Are you willing to repent from that and commit to upholding the entire Shari'ah as the Messenger brought it?
?
The author equates disregarding a clear text with atheism. Reflect on your own heart: do you ever feel that some rulings are outdated or unnecessary? What does that feeling reveal about your attachment to the Prophet's message? Will you hold firm to the clear texts even when they oppose your desires?

intermediate
10 min
Refuting the Claim of a Hidden Nass: Nasafi's Argument
Nasafi argues against the nass claim using tawatur and ijma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses the principles of tawatur and ijma to refute the Rafidi claim of an authentic but hidden nass for Ali's caliphate.

Core Idea
Widely transmitted traditions cannot be rejected without undermining all historical knowledge.

In this passage, Nasafi confronts the Rafidi argument that the explicit designation (nass) of Ali was authentic but suppressed. He turns the tables by comparing the alleged nass to well-known historical traditions that are universally accepted. If those traditions can be deemed unreliable, he argues, then no historical knowledge is safe. This lesson unpacks that powerful rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get its flow. Then read the translation. Notice how Nasafi uses a specific example to make his point.
2
Identify the two parts of the argument
The argument has two main moves: first, a comparison with a widely attested tradition; second, a reductio ad absurdum. Find where each begins and ends.
3
Understand the epistemological principles
Nasafi relies on the concepts of tawatur (widespread transmission) and ijma (consensus). Consider why these are important in establishing religious knowledge.
4
Reflect on the consequences of rejection
Nasafi warns that rejecting famous traditions leads to skepticism about even the existence of Ali and the Prophet. Why is this a powerful argument?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو زعموا أن ما ذكرتم من تزويجه بنته من عمر الله وما ذكرتم من مخاطبته إياهم بالخلافة وأمره المؤمنين وغير ذلك، ليس بثابت، قلنا: لو لم تثبت هذه الأحاديث مع اشتهارها وتداول أيدي النقلة إياها وتدوين أهل الأخبار إياها في المصنفات، واحتجاج أهل الفقه بنقل علي الله الله ابنته أم كلثوم من دار عمر الله بعد ما قتل إلى دار نفسه في جواز اعتداد المتوفى عنها زوجها في غير بيت زوجها عند تعذر الاعتداد في بيت زوجها كيف تثبت روايتكم النص على عليه، وهذا ما انعقد إجماع الأمة بخلافه، ولم يشتهر عند النقلة ولا رواه أحد من الأئمة حملة الآثار، ثم فيه ما فيه من تكذيب الله تعالى والطعن على علي الله أولاً ثم جمع الصحابة، ثم لو جاز كون مثل هذه الروايات باطلاً، لم يبق لهم على وجود على الله دليل، فضلاً عن خلافته، ولا على وجود الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم، والقول به تجاهل.
And if they claim that what you mentioned of his marrying his daughter to 'Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, and what you mentioned of his addressing them as caliph and his command to the believers, etc., is not established, we say: If these hadiths are not established despite their fame and the transmission of the narrators and the recording of historians in the compilations, and the argument of the jurists by the transmission of 'Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, of his daughter Umm Kulthum from the house of 'Umar after he was killed to his own house regarding the permissibility of the mourning period for a woman whose husband died outside her husband's house when it is impossible to observe the period in her husband's house, how then can your narration of the explicit text (nass) on 'Ali be established? This is something on which the consensus of the community has been concluded contrary to it, and it is not famous among transmitters, nor has any of the imams who carried the traditions narrated it. Moreover, it contains what it contains of the denial of Allah and the attack on 'Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, first, and then on the entire community of Companions. Then, if it is permissible for such narrations to be false, they would have no evidence for the existence of 'Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, let alone his caliphate, nor for the existence of the Messenger, peace be upon him, and saying this is ignorance.
Source pages p. 1129

Commentary
This passage presents a two-part argument. First, the author compares the nass narration with well-known historical traditions that are widely transmitted and used by jurists. He cites the example of 'Ali moving his daughter Umm Kulthum from 'Umar's house after 'Umar's death, which jurists use to derive a ruling on the waiting period ('iddah). If such widely transmitted traditions were considered unestablished, then the nass tradition—which is not widely transmitted and contradicts consensus—cannot be established either. Second, the author argues that accepting the possibility that such famous traditions are false would lead to skepticism about even the existence of 'Ali and the Prophet, which is absurd. This refutes the Rafidi claim that the nass was authentic but hidden.

Key Terms
نص
إجماع
تواتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The nass for Ali's caliphate was authentic but was hidden or suppressed by the majority.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the nass tradition lacks widespread transmission (tawatur) and contradicts consensus (ijma), making it unreliable, unlike the well-known traditions that are the basis of Islamic knowledge.

Checkpoint
What example does the author use to demonstrate a widely transmitted tradition that jurists rely on?
A) The marriage of 'Umar to Umm Kulthum
B) The hadith of Ghadir Khumm
C) The story of the Isra' and Mi'raj
D) The conquest of Khaybar

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the claim of a hidden nass by showing its incompatibility with widely transmitted traditions, and warns that rejecting such traditions leads to skepticism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When faced with a claim that contradicts widely accepted knowledge, do you examine your own sources with the same scrutiny Nasafi applied to the nass claim, or do you accept claims that feel convenient without checking their transmission? Will you commit to verifying the basis of what you believe today?
?
Nasafi warns that rejecting well-established traditions leads to doubt about the existence of the Prophet himself. Is there any area of your faith where you have allowed skepticism to creep in because of isolated reports? How will you reinforce your trust in the reliable sources of Islam?
?
The Rafidis accepted a tradition that undermined the consensus of the Companions. Are there beliefs you hold that isolate you from the broader Muslim community or contradict what the majority of scholars have agreed upon? Will you seek clarity and humility before Allah about this?

intermediate
8 min
Refuting Enmity Between Ali and the Companions
Nasafi refutes the claim of enmity between Ali and companions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that the claim of enmity between Ali and other companions is baseless and contrary to Islamic unity.

Core Idea
Islam removed deep-seated enmities; thus the claim against Ali is unfounded.

Nasafi begins by asserting that the alleged enmity between Ali and other companions is not historically established. He then dismantles specific arguments, such as the claim that companions held grudges due to relatives killed by Ali. Through examples of tribal unity and rhetorical questions, he shows the implausibility of the accusation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Nasafi uses a series of rhetorical questions and historical examples.
2
Identify the main claim being refuted
The claim is that there was an explicit text (nass) on Ali but it was concealed due to enmity. Understand what Nasafi is arguing against.
3
Examine the refutation of the killer relative argument
Nasafi argues that many companions killed disbelievers, not just Ali, so holding a grudge specifically against Ali is illogical.
He lists Umar, Talhah, and al-Zubayr as examples.
4
Study the example of Aws and Khazraj
The long-standing feud between these tribes was erased by Islam. This shows the companions' ability to overcome deep enmities, contradicting the claim of persistent hatred towards Ali.
The wars lasted nearly forty years in the Jahiliyyah.
5
Analyze the rhetorical questions about the Ansar
Nasafi asks: If there was enmity against Ali, why did the Ansar and supporters not abandon him after Uthman's murder when the right was supposedly his? This challenges the plausibility of the claim.
The question implies that true enmity would have prevented them from supporting Ali.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذه الوجوه يعرف بطلان قولهم: أنه كان نص عليه إلا أنه كتم للعداوة فإن العداوة بين علي الله وبين غيره من الصحابة - رضوان الله عليهم أجمعين لم تكن ثابتة. وما يزعمون من تحمل بعضهم عداوته لقتله أقاربهم من الكفرة، جهل محض فإن عليا والله لم يكن مختصا بقتل الكفار بل كان غيره من شجعان الصحابة كعمر وطلحة والزبير وغيرهم كلهم كانوا قتلوا الكفار، ثم متى كانت الصحابة يتحملون الضغائن ويطالبون بالأوتار الثابتة في الجاهلية، وقد ألف الله تعالى بين قلوبهم حتى ارتفعت الطوايل التي كانت بين قبيلتي الأنصار الأوس والخزرج في الجاهلية في الحروب التي كانت قامت بينهم قريبًا من أربعين سنة، المسماة وهي بحروب البغاث، ولم يبق من ذلك أثر حتى قبلوا الإسلام حتى تألفت قلوبهم، واتحدت كلمتهم بعد أن كانوا فرقتي تباين وحزبى تضاغن، فكيف اختص علي ببقاء تلك الأوتار في حقه دون غيره، ولو كان الأمر كذلك كيف لم يعدم الأنصار والأعوان بعد مقتل عثمان لا اله إذا كان الحق له ؟! وكيف لم تمنعهم تلك له؟! الأوتار والضغائن عن ذلك؟! وهب أنه قتل بعض أقارب من أسلم من أهل مكة فأي وتر كان لأهل المدينة
By these aspects, the falsehood of their statement becomes known: that there was an explicit text (nass) on him but it was concealed due to enmity. For the enmity between 'Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, and other companions – may Allah be pleased with them all – was not established. And what they claim, that some of them harbored enmity towards him because he killed their relatives among the disbelievers, is pure ignorance. For 'Ali, by Allah, was not singled out for killing disbelievers; rather, other brave companions like 'Umar, Talhah, and al-Zubayr, and others, all of them killed disbelievers. Then, when did the companions harbor grudges and seek blood-revenges established in the Jahiliyyah, when Allah had united their hearts so that the animosities that existed between the two tribes of the Ansar, al-Aws and al-Khazraj, in the Jahiliyyah, in the wars that arose between them for nearly forty years, called the Wars of al-Bughath, were removed, and no trace of that remained until they accepted Islam, and their hearts were united, and their word became one after they had been two conflicting parties and two rancorous groups? So how was 'Ali singled out for the continuation of those blood-revenges concerning him without others? And if it were so, why did the Ansar and supporters not fail after the murder of 'Uthman – there is no god – if the right was his? And how did those blood-revenges and grudges not prevent them from that? Grant that he killed some relatives of those who embraced Islam from the people of Makkah, what blood-revenge did the people of Madinah have?
Source pages p. 1130

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that the companions harbored enmity towards 'Ali, which supposedly led them to conceal the nass. The author makes several points: (1) Enmity between 'Ali and others is not historically established. (2) The claim that some companions disliked 'Ali because he killed their relatives in battle is baseless, since many companions killed disbelievers, not just 'Ali. (3) The companions had overcome intense tribal enmities, such as those between Aws and Khazraj that lasted nearly forty years, through Islam. If they could forgive such deep-seated hatred, they would hardly hold personal grudges against 'Ali. (4) The author raises rhetorical questions: if there was enmity against 'Ali, why did the Ansar and other supporters not abandon him after 'Uthman's murder when the caliphate was supposedly his? (5) Even if 'Ali killed some relatives of Meccans who later embraced Islam, the people of Madinah had no personal blood-revenge. The argument undermines the entire premise of concealed nass due to enmity.

Key Terms
عداوة
أوتار
الصحابة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is denying any differences among the companions.
Correction: The author is refuting the specific charge of persistent enmity against Ali, not denying all disagreements.

Checkpoint
What historical example does the author give to show that the companions were capable of overcoming old enmities?
A) The unity of Aws and Khazraj after Islam
B) The treaty of Hudaybiyyah
C) The conquest of Mecca
D) The battle of Badr

Key Takeaway
Nasafi argues that the strong unity among the companions, exemplified by the reconciliation of Aws and Khazraj, disproves any alleged enmity towards Ali.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a grudge against a fellow Muslim for a personal reason, even a small one? How does the example of the Aws and Khazraj, who set aside forty years of warfare for Islam, challenge you to examine whether your grudge is really justified? Will you take a concrete step toward reconciliation today?
?
The lesson shows that Islam can remove deep-seated enmities from the heart. Is there some enmity in your own heart—perhaps jealousy, bitterness, or resentment—that you have not surrendered to Allah? What specific action can you take this week to let go of that enmity and seek unity?
?
If the companions could put aside blood-revenges for the sake of Islam, what personal 'blood-revenge'—real or imagined—are you still carrying that contradicts the unity Islam demands? Will you ask Allah for the strength to release it now?

Sects and Disagreements
beginner
5 min
Refuting the Claim of Concealed Designation Due to Enmity
A refutation of the claim that nass was concealed due to enmity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that explicit designation (nass) of Ali was concealed due to enmity is false, based on historical and rational arguments.

Core Idea
No enmity existed among the Companions; Islam eradicated pre-Islamic grudges.

The author directly refutes a sectarian claim that the Prophet explicitly designated Ali as caliph, but that this was hidden because of enmity. He argues that such enmity is historically unproven and incompatible with the transformative effect of Islam on the Companions. This lesson unpacks his two-fold rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim being refuted
Begin by identifying the claim the author is responding to: that there was an explicit text (nass) designating Ali, but it was concealed due to enmity from other Companions.
This is a common sectarian argument that the author aims to dismantle.
2
Examine the first argument: no proven enmity
The author states that enmity between Ali and other Companions is not established. He dismisses the idea that Ali killing relatives of others caused enmity, because many Companions also killed disbelievers.
The author calls this claim 'pure ignorance' (jahal mahḍ).
3
Examine the second argument: Islam removed pre-Islamic grudges
The author points to the transformation of the Ansar tribes, al-Aws and al-Khazraj, who fought for forty years but became united in Islam. He argues that if such deep-seated rancors were removed, it is unlikely that enmity against Ali persisted.
This historical example shows that Islam eliminated old feuds, undermining the enmity argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذه الوجوه يعرف بطلان قولهم: أنه كان نص عليه إلا أنه كتم للعداوة فإن العداوة بين علي الله وبين غيره من الصحابة - رضوان الله عليهم أجمعين لم تكن ثابتة. وما يزعمون من تحمل بعضهم عداوته لقتله أقاربهم من الكفرة، جهل محض فإن عليا والله لم يكن مختصا بقتل الكفار بل كان غيره من شجعان الصحابة كعمر وطلحة والزبير وغيرهم كلهم كانوا قتلوا الكفار ، ثم متى كانت الصحابة يتحملون الضغائن ويطالبون بالأوتار الثابتة في الجاهلية، وقد ألف الله تعالى بين قلوبهم حتى ارتفعت الطوايل التي كانت بين قبيلتي الأنصار الأوس والخزرج في الجاهلية في الحروب التي كانت قامت بينهم قريبًا من أربعين سنة، المسماة وهي بحروب البغاث، ولم يبق من ذلك أثر حتى قبلوا الإسلام حتى تألفت قلوبهم، واتحدت كلمتهم بعد أن كانوا فرقتي تباين وحزبى تضاغن، فكيف اختص علي ببقاء تلك الأوتار في حقه دون غيره
And by these aspects the falsity of their statement is known: that there was an explicit designation (nass) upon him except that it was concealed due to enmity. For enmity between Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, and others among the Companions — may Allah be pleased with them all — was not established. And their claim that some of them bore enmity towards him because he killed their relatives from among the disbelievers is pure ignorance. Indeed, Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, was not singled out for killing disbelievers; rather, other brave Companions like Umar, Talha, al-Zubayr, and others all killed disbelievers. Then when were the Companions accustomed to harboring grudges and demanding blood‑revenge (awtār) established in the pre‑Islamic period, when Allah Almighty had composed their hearts until the long‑standing rancors (ṭawāʿil) that existed between the two tribes of the Anṣār, al‑Aws and al‑Khazraj, in the pre‑Islamic period during the wars that took place between them for nearly forty years, called the wars of al‑Bughāth, were removed, and no trace of that remained, until they accepted Islam and their hearts became united and their word became one after they had been two opposing parties and two hostile groups? So how was ʿAlī singled out for the persistence of those blood‑feuds against him rather than others?
Source pages p. 1130

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Shīʿī claim that the Prophet explicitly designated ʿAlī as caliph (nass) but that this designation was concealed due to enmity between ʿAlī and other Companions. The author first asserts that no such enmity is established. The claim that some Companions bore enmity because ʿAlī killed their relatives is dismissed as pure ignorance (jahal mahḍ), since many Companions participated in killing disbelievers. The author then argues that the Companions, having been transformed by Islam from pre‑Islamic blood‑feuds (awtār, ṭawāʿil) into a united community, would not have harbored grudges. The example of the Anṣār tribes of al‑Aws and al‑Khazraj, who fought for forty years and then became brothers in Islam, is cited. The rhetorical question concludes: if such deep‑seated rancors were removed, how could ʿAlī be singled out for lingering enmity?

Key Terms
نص (nass)
عداوة (enmity)
أوتار (blood‑feuds)
طوايل (long‑standing rancors)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Companions harbored enmity against Ali because he killed their relatives.
Correction: Ali was not alone in killing disbelievers; many Companions did. Moreover, Islam eradicated all pre-Islamic grudges, as seen with the Ansar tribes.

Checkpoint
What does the author use as evidence that enmity could not have led to concealing the nass on ʿAlī?
A) ʿAlī was not involved in killing disbelievers.
B) The Anṣār of al‑Aws and al‑Khazraj abandoned their pre‑Islamic hostilities after accepting Islam.
C) The Prophet explicitly forbade holding grudges against ʿAlī.
D) The Companions never fought in wars.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the claim of concealed nass by denying established enmity and demonstrating that Islam eliminated pre-Islamic grudges, making the enmity argument baseless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a grudge against a fellow Muslim, how does that contradict the transformation Islam brought to the Companions? What specific grudge are you still nurturing, and what would it take to release it as the Aws and Khazraj released theirs?
?
If Islam could erase forty years of bloodshed between two tribes, what excuse do you have for not reconciling with someone you have a conflict with? What concrete step will you take this week to heal that relationship?
?
The author calls the claim of enmity toward Ali 'pure ignorance.' Can you identify a belief you hold about a group of Muslims that might be based on hearsay or sectarian narratives rather than evidence? How can you verify it with the same critical approach this lesson uses?

The Evidence from ʿAlī's Conduct and the Anṣār's Compliance
Author argues from ʿAlī's conduct and the Anṣār's compliance with 'al-a'imma min Quraysh'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses ʿAlī's own actions and the Anṣār's reaction to refute the claim of an explicit designation (nass) for ʿAlī.

Core Idea
ʿAlī's silence at Saqīfa proves there was no explicit designation.

This passage presents a key argument from classical Sunnī theology against the Shīʿī claim that the Prophet explicitly designated ʿAlī as his successor. The author examines two pieces of evidence: the behavior of the Anṣār, who accepted a general statement (al-a'imma min Quraysh) but not a specific nass for ʿAlī, and ʿAlī's own conduct during his caliphate and after Saqīfa. By analyzing these, the author concludes that ʿAlī had no knowledge of any explicit text or will.

Guided Path
1
Understand the hypothetical opening
The author begins with a conditional scenario about ʿAlī killing Meccan relatives, noting the Anṣār had no blood-feud. This sets up the argument that the Anṣār had no personal grievance against ʿAlī.
2
Examine the Anṣār's response at Saqīfa
The Anṣār accepted the Prophet's hadith 'al-a'imma min Quraysh' but did not accept a specific nass for ʿAlī. This shows they followed a general designation, not a specific one for ʿAlī.
3
Analyze ʿAlī's silence during his caliphate
The author asks why ʿAlī, with many supporters, did not narrate the alleged nass against his opponents. This silence is evidence that no explicit designation existed.
4
Consider the courage argument and the promise about oath-breakers
It is inconceivable that ʿAlī, known for his courage, would cowardly surrender his right. Moreover, the Prophet promised he would kill the oath-breakers, implying he would survive to assert his right. His surrender indicates he knew of no nass.
5
Draw the conclusion
The author concludes that ʿAlī surrendered the caliphate because he had no knowledge of any covenant, explicit designation, or will from the Prophet.
This is the key point of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهب أنه قتل بعض أقارب من أسلم من أهل مكة فأي وتر كان لأهل المدينة من الأوس والخزرج ولم يعاونوه ولم يقبلوا النص عليه ولم(1) ينقادوا لذلك، وقد انقادوا لقول النبي عليه السلام: الأئمة من قريش حين روى لهم يوم سقيفة بني ساعدة، وكيف لم يرو على الله ذلك النص زمان خلافته وكثرة أتباعه وشيعته ومحبيه على من نابذه وناصبه الحرب؟ وكيف يتوهم منه تسليم ما هو أحق به من غيره إلى من ليس بأهل له جبنًا منه وفشلاً، والشجاعة شجعته والاعتماد على قول الرسول عليه السلام اعتماده والثقة بذلك ثقته، وقد وعده النبي عليه السلام أنه يقتل الناكثين وفيه إيقاع الأمن له عن القتل ونزول البلاء به دون مضي ذلك على يده فإذا كان سلم الأمر ، دل أنه إنما أسلم لأنه لم يعرف لنفسه من الرسول عليه السلام إليه عهد أو لا ثبت له منه نص فيه أو وصية.
Suppose that he killed some relatives of those from Mecca who had embraced Islam — what blood‑feud was there for the people of Medina from al‑Aws and al‑Khazraj? They did not help him, did not accept the explicit designation (nass) for him, nor submit to it, while they had submitted to the statement of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'The leaders are from Quraysh' when he narrated it to them on the day of Saqīfat Banī Sāʿida. And why did ʿAlī, may Allah be pleased with him, not narrate that explicit designation during his caliphate, when his followers, partisans, and lovers were numerous against those who opposed and fought him? And how could it be imagined of him that he would surrender what he was more entitled to than others to one who was not worthy of it, out of cowardice and weakness, while he was courageous, his reliance was on the statement of the Messenger, peace be upon him, and his trust was in that? The Prophet, peace be upon him, had promised him that he would kill the oath‑breakers (al‑nākithīn), and in that promise there was assurance of safety from killing and affliction befalling him without it being carried out by his hand. So if he surrendered the matter, it indicates that he only surrendered because he did not know of any covenant from the Messenger, peace be upon him, to him, or that there was not established for him from him an explicit designation or a will.
Source pages p. 1130

Commentary
This passage presents a series of arguments from ʿAlī's own conduct and the behavior of the Anṣār. The author begins with a hypothetical: even if ʿAlī killed some Meccan relatives, what blood‑feud did the Medinan Anṣār have? The Anṣār did not support ʿAlī or accept a nass for him, but they did accept the Prophet's statement 'al‑a'imma min Quraysh' at Saqīfa. This shows they followed a general designation, not a specific one for ʿAlī. The author then asks why ʿAlī, during his caliphate and with many supporters, did not narrate the alleged nass against his opponents. It is inconceivable that ʿAlī, known for his courage, would cowardly surrender his right to someone unworthy. Furthermore, the Prophet promised ʿAlī that he would kill the oath‑breakers (al‑nākithīn), which implies he would survive to do so. Since ʿAlī surrendered the caliphate earlier (at Saqīfa), this indicates he had no knowledge of any explicit text or will from the Prophet regarding his succession.

Key Terms
نص (explicit designation)
سقيفة بني ساعدة (Saqīfat Banī Sāʿida)
الناكثين (oath‑breakers)
وصية (will)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think ʿAlī's surrender at Saqīfa was due to cowardice or fear of the Anṣār.
Correction: The author argues ʿAlī was courageous and trusted the Prophet's promise; his surrender was because he had no explicit designation from the Prophet.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that ʿAlī's surrender of the caliphate at Saqīfa proves there was no explicit designation?
A) Because ʿAlī was afraid of the Anṣār.
B) Because ʿAlī did not know about the hadith 'al‑a'imma min Quraysh'.
C) Because ʿAlī was promised he would kill the oath‑breakers, implying he would live to fight later, and he would not have surrendered a clear right.
D) Because the Anṣār rejected ʿAlī outright.

Key Takeaway
The author demonstrates that ʿAlī's actions are incompatible with the existence of an explicit designation for him, thereby refuting a central Shīʿī claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do I cling to a belief I hold dear, even when the evidence from the very person involved contradicts it? Am I willing to accept that ʿAlī's own actions might disprove what I wish were true?
?
If I were in ʿAlī's position, knowing I had the truth but facing opposition, would I have the courage to remain silent if there was no divine command to speak? What does that say about my reliance on God's plan over my own desires?
?
The Anṣār submitted to a general principle from the Prophet, even though it meant their own candidate was not chosen. When a prophetic teaching goes against my personal preference or group interest, do I submit, or do I seek ways to reinterpret it?

Impossibility of Concealing a Widespread Hadith
Author argues that a widely known hadith cannot be concealed, and states his motive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a widely known hadith cannot be concealed by a group, and how the author uses this argument to defend Abu Bakr's caliphate.

Core Idea
A widespread hadith cannot be hidden; to think otherwise is profound ignorance.

This passage presents a key argument from Imam al-Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla. He argues that a hadith that was widely transmitted among the Companions could not have been concealed, because collusion to hide it is impossible. He then explains his reason for writing this refutation: to counter attacks on Abu Bakr's caliphate. Let's examine the logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first the argument about concealment, then the author's motive.
Pay attention to the rhetorical question at the beginning.
2
Understand the argument
The author uses 'normal customs' to assert that concealment of a widespread thing is impossible. Why? Because collusion to hide it is unimaginable.
3
Consider the implication
If concealment were possible, then widespread falsehood could also be accepted. This shows the absurdity of the opposing view.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Connect to historical context
The author mentions that attackers on Abu Bakr's caliphate use this to undermine religion. This is why he writes this refutation.
The term 'al-Ṣiddīq' refers to Abu Bakr.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Do you see how this argument protects the reliability of hadith transmission? The author's logical move is to appeal to common sense and impossibility of collusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم هل يتوهم في العادات الجارية كتمان حديث اشتهر وثبت في قوم لا يتصور تواطؤهم على ذلك، ولو جاز هذا لجاز اشتهار الكذب وروايته عن قوم هذا وصفهم، وهذا كله جهالة لا يدانيها جهالة. ولولا تفاقم الأمر في زماننا هذا من الطاعنين على الصديق صلي الله ونفاة إمامته الجاعلين ذلك ذريعة إلى ما يرومون من إبطال الدين وإثبات الإلحاد لما بالغت كل هذه المبالغة في ذلك، لظهوره عوار على من له أدنى لب والله الموفق.
Then is it conceivable according to normal customs to conceal a hadith that became widespread and established among a people whose collusion upon that is unimaginable? And if this were permissible, then the widespread reporting of falsehood by people with such description would be permissible. And all of this is ignorance that no ignorance approaches. And were it not that the matter has become grave in our time, because of those who attack al‑Ṣiddīq (Abu Bakr), may Allah be pleased with him, and deny his imamate, making that a means to what they aim for of nullifying the religion and establishing atheism, I would not have gone to such lengths in this, for its deficiency is apparent to anyone with the least intelligence. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 1131

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the impossibility of concealing a widely known hadith, and it also explains the author's reason for writing. The author states that it is inconceivable, according to normal customs, that a hadith which became widespread and established among a people (the Companions) could be concealed, because collusion to hide it is unimaginable. If such concealment were allowed, then widespread falsehood could also be accepted. This is described as extreme ignorance. The author then says that were it not for the severity of the attacks in his time against Abu Bakr (al‑Ṣiddīq) and those who deny his caliphate, using that as a pretext to undermine religion and promote atheism, he would not have written such a detailed refutation, since the weakness of their argument is obvious to anyone with intelligence. He ends with a prayer: 'Allah is the Grantor of success.'

Key Terms
كتمان
حديث مشتهر
تواطؤ
الصديق
إلحاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that a conspiracy could suppress a widely known truth.
Correction: The author argues that normal customs and the impossibility of collusion prevent the concealment of a widespread hadith.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say it is impossible to conceal a widespread hadith?
A) Because the hadith was written down immediately.
B) Because collusion to conceal it is unimaginable.
C) Because the Prophet forbade its concealment.
D) Because there were many narrators.

Key Takeaway
A widely transmitted hadith cannot be concealed because collusion to hide it is impossible; to claim otherwise is profound ignorance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a well-known Islamic teaching simply because you hadn't personally heard it before? What does this passage teach you about the reliability of widely transmitted knowledge from the Companions?
?
The author wrote this refutation because the attacks on Abu Bakr were growing in his time. Are you aware of any modern claims that try to undermine the legitimacy of the first caliph? How can this argument equip you to respond with certainty?
?
This passage calls the denial of the widespread hadith 'ignorance that no ignorance approaches.' Is there an area of your faith where you have been influenced by doubts or biases instead of looking at clear proofs? What action will you take today to seek the truth about the imamate of Abu Bakr and the integrity of the Sunnah?

The Prophet's Guidance on Leadership: Evidence, Not Explicit Text
Author refutes Shīʿī claim about prophetic neglect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that the Prophet would have neglected the ummah by not explicitly designating an imam, and what alternative guidance the Prophet provided.

Core Idea
The Prophet guided the ummah through evidence, not just explicit text.

This passage tackles a key Shīʿī objection: that if the Prophet did not explicitly name a successor, he would have abandoned the community. The author declares this false and explains that the Prophet gave clear indications (dilāla) about who was most entitled. This sets up a distinction between explicit designation (nass) and implicit evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author begins by stating the claim in compact form.
2
Identify the two parts of the argument
First, the author presents the false claim. Then he refutes it and offers a positive alternative. Find where the shift occurs.
3
Reflect on the key distinction
What is the difference between nass (explicit text) and dilāla (evidence)? Why does the author insist that the Prophet did not neglect the community?
This distinction is crucial for the broader discussion on imamate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لو لم ينص على إمام لضيع الأمة. قول باطل] وما يزعمون أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لو لم ينص على إمامة أحد لضيع أعظم أمور الأمة وأوقعهم في أفحش فتنة، وهذا لا يليق به عليه السلام. كلام باطل، فإن النبي عليه السلام قد أقام الدلالة على الأحق بذلك لكن لا بطريق النص على ما نبين،
[The claim that] if he had not designated an imam, he would have neglected the community. That is a false statement. And what they claim — that if the Prophet, peace be upon him, had not designated anyone for the imamate, he would have neglected the greatest of the community's affairs and thrown them into the worst sedition, and this is not befitting him, peace be upon him — is false speech. For the Prophet, peace be upon him, indeed established evidence for the one most entitled to that, but not through explicit designation (nass), as we will explain.
Source pages p. 1131

Commentary
This passage refutes the Shīʿī argument that if the Prophet did not explicitly designate an imam, he would have neglected the community's greatest affair and led them into terrible sedition, which is unworthy of him. The author declares this a false statement (qawl bāṭil). He asserts that the Prophet did provide guidance (dilāla) on who is most entitled to leadership, but not through explicit text (nass), as the author promises to explain further. The lesson ends mid‑sentence, indicating that more follows in the next pages.

Key Terms
نص (explicit designation)
إمامة (imamate)
دلالة (evidence/indication)
فتنة (sedition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that only an explicit text (nass) can prevent the Prophet from neglecting the community.
Correction: The author argues that implicit evidence (dilāla) suffices and the Prophet did provide such guidance.

Checkpoint
What does the author say the Prophet provided instead of an explicit text for the imamate?
A) A direct command.
B) Evidence (dilāla) for the most entitled person.
C) No guidance at all.
D) A written will.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet did not neglect the ummah; he provided evidence for leadership without explicit text.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You demand explicit instructions from others before acting? Where in your life do you rely on clear-cut texts from the Qur'an and Sunnah while ignoring the broader evidence and wisdom they contain? How can you train yourself to see the guidance in subtle indications?
?
The Shīʿī claim assumes the Prophet would neglect the community if he didn't leave an explicit successor. Do you ever assume that God or His Prophet have not provided enough guidance for a situation in your life? What does that reveal about your trust in the completeness of the religion?
?
If evidence (dilāla) suffices for the greatest affair of the ummah, then what excuses do you have for ignoring the clear evidence in your own life—like the signs of Allah, the sunnah of the Prophet, and the advice of scholars? When will you act on what is already clear instead of waiting for a direct command?

Refutation of the Necessity of Explicit Textual Designation for the Imam
Refutation of the necessity of explicit text for imamate and defense of dalala.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Rafidah claim that the Prophet must have explicitly designated an imam is false, and that evidence (dalala) and rational reflection suffice.

Core Idea
The Prophet's guidance is not limited to explicit texts; evidence and reason are sufficient.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi refutes the Shia claim that the Prophet's silence on naming a successor would be a neglect of the community's welfare. He argues that providing evidence (dalala) is enough, and that divine wisdom includes testing through reflection, not just explicit commands.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of the Rafidah claim
Start by reading the Arabic and translation of the opening lines, where the author states the Rafidah claim and then dismisses it as false speech.
Notice how the author immediately counters the claim by asserting that the Prophet did provide evidence.
2
Understand the concept of dalala vs nass
The author explains that the Prophet established evidence (dalala) for who is most deserving, not an explicit text (nass). Reflect on the difference between these two types of guidance.
Dalala is a sign or indication that requires reasoning, while nass is a direct statement.
3
Recognize the broader principle about shariah
The author states that the method of clarifying laws is not confined to nass; it includes testing and diversity of opinion. Consider how this principle applies to other areas of Islamic law.
This principle is key to Sunni thought on ijtihad and the role of reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يزعمون أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لو لم ينص على إمامة أحد لضيع أعظم أمور الأمة وأوقعهم في أفحش فتنة، وهذا لا يليق به عليه السلام. كلام باطل، فإن النبي عليه السلام قد أقام الدلالة على الأحق بذلك لكن لا بطريق النص على ما نبين، وليس طريق بيان الشرائع بمقصور على النص ليقال إنه لو لم ينص فقد ضيع أمر الأمة بل من الحكمة إيجاب الامتحان والتكليف بوجوه يختلف فيها أولو الألباب كما في سائر المواضع التي لا نص فيها، أليس أن كل ذي عقل يعقل بالضرورة حدوث نفسه ثم يعرف بالتدبير والتأمل حدوث كل محسوس لم يشهد ابتداءه، ثم يعقل كون كل حدوث بمحدث أحدثه بفضل تدبر فيه ونظر، ثم يعرف المحدث بعد طول النظر في أمره أنه يوصف بأنه ما هو أجسم أم عرض أم غير جسم ولا عرض؟ ويوصف بالعلم في الأزل أو الحدوث، وكذا القدرة وجميع الصفات ثم معرفة الرسل والأنبياء عليهم السلام، ثم معرفة صدق الأخبار وكذبها، ثم قبول الشهادات وردها. إن ذلك كله أو تمامه كان طرق الحق في ذلك من الباطل النظر والفكر مع التضرع إلى رب العالمين في التوفيق لذلك والهداية إليه فإنه الجواد الكريم، وإذا ثبت ذا لم يكن لأحد منع جواز محنة الصحابة بعد قبضه عليه السلام بمعرفة الأصلح للخلافة، والعقد له بما سبق من أدلة نظره للمسلمين وشفقته عليهم وتعظيمه أمر الله تعالى وقلة مبالاته في ذات الله تعالى بلومة اللائمين وطعن الملحدين واهتمامه لما عليه من أمر المؤمنين، وليست الروافض بأول طائفة من أهل الزيغ جحدت أمرًا قصرت عقولهم عن الوقوف على وجه الحكمة فيه، غير أنهم الجهالة اختصوا بالطعن على الصحابة، وعلى من أرادوا إثبات الحق له وعلى سلف الأمة بل على الله تعالى وعلى رسوله ما قررنا وبالله العصمة والنجاة عن كل ضلالة وبدعة.
And what they claim that the Prophet, peace be upon him, if he had not designated an imam for anyone, he would have neglected the greatest of the community's affairs and caused them to fall into the most heinous trial, and this is not befitting of him, peace be upon him. [This is] false speech. Indeed, the Prophet, peace be upon him, established evidence (al-dalala) for the one most deserving of that, but not by way of explicit text (al-nass), as we will explain. And the way of clarifying the laws is not confined to explicit text so that it could be said that if he had not designated, he would have neglected the community's affair. Rather, it is from wisdom to require testing and obligation in ways that the people of understanding differ over, as in other matters where there is no explicit text. Is it not that every possessor of intellect necessarily understands the contingency (huduth) of his own self, then knows by reflection and contemplation the contingency of every perceived thing whose beginning he did not witness, then understands that every contingent thing is brought into existence by an originator who originated it by virtue of reflection and consideration in it, then knows the originator after lengthy consideration of his affair, that he is described as to whether he is a body or an accident or neither body nor accident? And he is described as having knowledge in eternity or as having originated knowledge, and similarly power and all attributes. Then [comes] knowledge of the messengers and prophets, peace be upon them, then knowledge of the truthfulness of reports and their falsehood, then acceptance of testimonies and rejection of them. All of that, or its completion, was that the paths of truth in that from falsehood are reflection (al-nazar) and thought (al-fikr) together with supplication to the Lord of the worlds for success in that and guidance to it, for He is the Generous, the Bountiful. And when this is established, it is not permissible for anyone to forbid the testing (mihna) of the Companions after his departure, peace be upon him, through knowing the most suitable for the caliphate, and the pledge to him based on preceding evidences of his care for the Muslims and his compassion for them and his magnifying the command of Allah Almighty and his disregard in the essence of Allah Almighty for the blame of blamers and the slander of heretics and his concern for the affairs of the believers. And the Rawafid are not the first sect among the people of deviation who denied a matter that their intellects fell short of grasping the aspect of wisdom in it, but they, in their ignorance, specialized in slandering the Companions, and those whom they wish to establish the truth for, and the predecessors of the community, indeed Allah Almighty and His Messenger, as we have stated. And with Allah is protection and salvation from every error and innovation.
Source pages p. 1131

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Rafidah (Shia) claim that the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) must have explicitly designated an imam (caliph) by name (nass), and that failing to do so would have been a neglect of the community's most important affair. The author, Imam al-Nasafi, argues that this claim is false because the Prophet did provide evidence (dalala) for who was most deserving, though not through an explicit textual designation. He then makes a broader epistemological point: the way of clarifying religious laws is not restricted to explicit texts; it is part of divine wisdom to impose testing and obligation through means that require reflection and differing opinions among people of understanding, as is the case in many other matters where no explicit text exists. To illustrate, the author draws an analogy with how humans come to know God and His attributes through reason: a person necessarily knows the contingency of his own soul, then through reflection comes to know the contingency of all perceived things, then infers the existence of a Creator, then after prolonged consideration determines His attributes (whether He is a body, accident, or neither; whether His knowledge is eternal or originated, etc.). Then through further reflection one comes to know the prophets, the truthfulness of reports, and the acceptance or rejection of testimonies. All of this is achieved through reflection (nazar) and thought (fikr) combined with supplication to God. The author concludes that if this rational method is valid for knowing God, it is also valid for the Companions to determine the most suitable caliph through their own reasoned consideration, guided by the Prophet's care and compassion. He then criticizes the Rawafid as a deviant sect that denies matters beyond their intellectual capacity and specializes in slandering the Companions and even God and His Messenger.

Key Terms
نص (nass)
إمامة (imama)
دلالة (dalala)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the absence of an explicit text (nass) for the imam means the Prophet neglected the community.
Correction: The author clarifies that evidence (dalala) and rational reflection suffice, and shariah is not confined to explicit texts.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what did the Prophet provide to indicate the most deserving person for imamate?
A) An explicit text (nass) naming the person
B) Evidence (dalala) but not an explicit text
C) A miracle to indicate the person
D) Silence, leaving it to the community

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's guidance regarding leadership includes providing evidence, not necessarily explicit naming; this allows for rational reflection and testing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever demanded explicit guidance from Allah regarding a major decision in your life, assuming that without it you are neglected? How does this lesson's claim that Allah's wisdom includes providing evidence and testing your intellect challenge you to respond to uncertainty with active seeking rather than passive waiting?
?
The Rafidah argued that without an explicit text the community would fall into terrible fitna. Yet the Prophet, in his wisdom, left room for ijtihad and difference. Are you willing to take responsibility for your own understanding of Islam, trusting that the Prophet's guidance is sufficient even when not spelled out? What area of your faith have you been clinging to a demand for a clear 'yes/no' instead of applying your mind to the evidence?
?
This passage teaches that it is from divine wisdom to test people through matters where people of understanding differ. How does that change your attitude towards scholarly disagreements? Will you now see them as a test of your sincerity and humility, or will you continue to dismiss differing opinions as innovation? Commit to one concrete step you will take to engage with a differing view with patience and intellectual honesty.

The Rational Method: Nazar and Fikr as Paths to Truth
Analogy of rational knowledge leading to the permissibility of the Companions' testing (mihna).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the rational methods of nazar (reflection) and fikr (thought) lead to knowledge of God and truth, and how this applies to the Companions' selection of the caliph.

Core Idea
Reason and reflection are divinely sanctioned paths to truth, even for choosing a leader.

Imam al-Nasafi provides a detailed analogy of how humans come to know God through contemplation, arguing that the same rational method can be applied to determine the most suitable caliph after the Prophet's death. He then criticizes the Rawafid for denying such reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy of rational knowledge
Begin by reading the long rhetorical question that traces the steps from knowing one's own contingency to knowing the Creator and His attributes.
This analogy is meant to show that reason is a valid tool for arriving at truth.
2
Identify the stages of reasoning
List the stages mentioned: (1) contingency of self, (2) contingency of the world, (3) inference of a Creator, (4) attributes of the Creator, (5) knowledge of prophets, (6) truthfulness of reports, (7) acceptance of testimonies.
Each stage builds on the previous one through reflection and thought.
3
Connect to the Companions' situation
The author then applies this reasoning to the Companions: if these rational stages are valid, then it is permissible for the Companions to use their reason to determine the best caliph after the Prophet's death.
This refutes the Rafidah claim that the Companions acted without divine guidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يزعمون أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لو لم ينص على إمامة أحد لضيع أعظم أمور الأمة وأوقعهم في أفحش فتنة، وهذا لا يليق به عليه السلام. كلام باطل، فإن النبي عليه السلام قد أقام الدلالة على الأحق بذلك لكن لا بطريق النص على ما نبين، وليس طريق بيان الشرائع بمقصور على النص ليقال إنه لو لم ينص فقد ضيع أمر الأمة بل من الحكمة إيجاب الامتحان والتكليف بوجوه يختلف فيها أولو الألباب كما في سائر المواضع التي لا نص فيها، أليس أن كل ذي عقل يعقل بالضرورة حدوث نفسه ثم يعرف بالتدبير والتأمل حدوث كل محسوس لم يشهد ابتداءه، ثم يعقل كون كل حدوث بمحدث أحدثه بفضل تدبر فيه ونظر، ثم يعرف المحدث بعد طول النظر في أمره أنه يوصف بأنه ما هو أجسم أم عرض أم غير جسم ولا عرض؟ ويوصف بالعلم في الأزل أو الحدوث، وكذا القدرة وجميع الصفات ثم معرفة الرسل والأنبياء عليهم السلام، ثم معرفة صدق الأخبار وكذبها، ثم قبول الشهادات وردها. إن ذلك كله أو تمامه كان طرق الحق في ذلك من الباطل النظر والفكر مع التضرع إلى رب العالمين في التوفيق لذلك والهداية إليه فإنه الجواد الكريم، وإذا ثبت ذا لم يكن لأحد منع جواز محنة الصحابة بعد قبضه عليه السلام بمعرفة الأصلح للخلافة، والعقد له بما سبق من أدلة نظره للمسلمين وشفقته عليهم وتعظيمه أمر الله تعالى وقلة مبالاته في ذات الله تعالى بلومة اللائمين وطعن الملحدين واهتمامه لما عليه من أمر المؤمنين، وليست الروافض بأول طائفة من أهل الزيغ جحدت أمرًا قصرت عقولهم عن الوقوف على وجه الحكمة فيه، غير أنهم الجهالة اختصوا بالطعن على الصحابة، وعلى من أرادوا إثبات الحق له وعلى سلف الأمة بل على الله تعالى وعلى رسوله ما قررنا وبالله العصمة والنجاة عن كل ضلالة وبدعة.
And what they claim that the Prophet, peace be upon him, if he had not designated an imam for anyone, he would have neglected the greatest of the community's affairs and caused them to fall into the most heinous trial, and this is not befitting of him, peace be upon him. [This is] false speech. Indeed, the Prophet, peace be upon him, established evidence (al-dalala) for the one most deserving of that, but not by way of explicit text (al-nass), as we will explain. And the way of clarifying the laws is not confined to explicit text so that it could be said that if he had not designated, he would have neglected the community's affair. Rather, it is from wisdom to require testing and obligation in ways that the people of understanding differ over, as in other matters where there is no explicit text. Is it not that every possessor of intellect necessarily understands the contingency (huduth) of his own self, then knows by reflection and contemplation the contingency of every perceived thing whose beginning he did not witness, then understands that every contingent thing is brought into existence by an originator who originated it by virtue of reflection and consideration in it, then knows the originator after lengthy consideration of his affair, that he is described as to whether he is a body or an accident or neither body nor accident? And he is described as having knowledge in eternity or as having originated knowledge, and similarly power and all attributes. Then [comes] knowledge of the messengers and prophets, peace be upon them, then knowledge of the truthfulness of reports and their falsehood, then acceptance of testimonies and rejection of them. All of that, or its completion, was that the paths of truth in that from falsehood are reflection (al-nazar) and thought (al-fikr) together with supplication to the Lord of the worlds for success in that and guidance to it, for He is the Generous, the Bountiful. And when this is established, it is not permissible for anyone to forbid the testing (mihna) of the Companions after his departure, peace be upon him, through knowing the most suitable for the caliphate, and the pledge to him based on preceding evidences of his care for the Muslims and his compassion for them and his magnifying the command of Allah Almighty and his disregard in the essence of Allah Almighty for the blame of blamers and the slander of heretics and his concern for the affairs of the believers. And the Rawafid are not the first sect among the people of deviation who denied a matter that their intellects fell short of grasping the aspect of wisdom in it, but they, in their ignorance, specialized in slandering the Companions, and those whom they wish to establish the truth for, and the predecessors of the community, indeed Allah Almighty and His Messenger, as we have stated. And with Allah is protection and salvation from every error and innovation.
Source pages p. 1131

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Rafidah (Shia) claim that the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) must have explicitly designated an imam (caliph) by name (nass), and that failing to do so would have been a neglect of the community's most important affair. The author, Imam al-Nasafi, argues that this claim is false because the Prophet did provide evidence (dalala) for who was most deserving, though not through an explicit textual designation. He then makes a broader epistemological point: the way of clarifying religious laws is not restricted to explicit texts; it is part of divine wisdom to impose testing and obligation through means that require reflection and differing opinions among people of understanding, as is the case in many other matters where no explicit text exists. To illustrate, the author draws an analogy with how humans come to know God and His attributes through reason: a person necessarily knows the contingency of his own soul, then through reflection comes to know the contingency of all perceived things, then infers the existence of a Creator, then after prolonged consideration determines His attributes (whether He is a body, accident, or neither; whether His knowledge is eternal or originated, etc.). Then through further reflection one comes to know the prophets, the truthfulness of reports, and the acceptance or rejection of testimonies. All of this is achieved through reflection (nazar) and thought (fikr) combined with supplication to God. The author concludes that if this rational method is valid for knowing God, it is also valid for the Companions to determine the most suitable caliph through their own reasoned consideration, guided by the Prophet's care and compassion. He then criticizes the Rawafid as a deviant sect that denies matters beyond their intellectual capacity and specializes in slandering the Companions and even God and His Messenger.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar)
فكر (fikr)
حدوث (huduth)
محدث (muhdith)
محنة (mihna)
روافض (Rawafid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first thing a rational person necessarily understands?
A) The existence of God
B) The contingency of his own self
C) The truthfulness of prophets
D) The attributes of the Creator

Key Takeaway
The rational method of nazar and fikr is a divinely ordained path to truth, and it legitimizes the Companions' use of reason to choose a caliph.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult decision in your faith or life, do you truly engage your intellect in reflection and supplication, or do you rely on habit and the opinions of others without personal reasoning? What would change if you took the author's method of nazar and fikr seriously today?
?
The Rawafid are criticized for rejecting what their minds could not grasp. Is there any aspect of your faith—a verse, a hadith, or a historical event—that you have dismissed because it seemed unreasonable? Are you willing to humble yourself and seek deeper understanding through patient reflection and prayer?
?
The Companions used their reason to choose a leader for the Muslim community after the Prophet's death, and the author defends this as a legitimate application of the rational method. Have you ever used your intellect to evaluate the leaders you follow—whether in religion, politics, or community? If not, what is holding you back from reasoning carefully about those who guide you?

The Method of Choosing the Imam by Selection
Al-Nasafi argues for imamah by choice and explains the contracting process.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the imamah is established by choice and not by explicit text, and how a single qualified person can contract the imamah.

Core Idea
The imamah is established by choice, and a single valid contract suffices.

Al-Nasafi argues that since the imamah is not established by explicit text (nass), it must be established by choice (ikhtiyar). He then presents the view of al-Ash'ari that a single qualified person can contract the imamah, and supports this with examples from the early Muslim community, showing that universal consensus is not required.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
Pay attention to how al-Nasafi structures his reasoning.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage states that the imamah is established by choice, not by text. Mark this as the central thesis.
3
Examine the rejection of consensus requirement
Note the arguments against requiring ijma': it causes delay, the Companions did not require it, and numbers do not provide preponderance.
The passage uses the example of Saqifa to illustrate this.
4
Analyze the historical evidence
Look at the two examples: 'Umar's proposal to pledge to Abu 'Ubaydah and Abu Bakr's appointment of 'Umar. Both show that a single contract suffices.
These examples are used to demonstrate the lack of need for universal consensus.
5
Consider al-Juwayni's addition
Note that al-Juwayni adds the requirement of witnesses to prevent secret claims, drawing an analogy to marriage.
This is an extra condition not mentioned by al-Ash'ari.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكيفية التي يتم بها اختيار الإمام ثم إذا بطل ثبوت الخلافة بالنص ثبت أنها تثبت بالاختيار ثم للناس كلام في كيفية ذلك ولم أقف فيه على مذهب مشايخنا رحمهم الله تعالى وروى عن الأشعري أن واحدا من أهل الاجتهاد لو اجتهد في زمان موت إمام ورأى أسباب الصلاحية في أحد ثابتة فعقد له الإمامة انعقدت له وليس لغيره بعد ذلك أن يخالفه ولا وجه إلى اشتراط الإجماع اليوم لما فيه من تأخير الإمامة عن وقت الحاجة إليها لو شرط فيها الإجماع على أن الصحابة لم يشترطوا فيها الإجماع عند الاختيار والمبايعة وإنما اعتبروا وجود العقد ثم أوجبوا المبايعة بعد ذلك ثم الإجماع إذا خرج من أن يكون شرطا لم يكن عدد أولي من عدد فسقط اعتباره لثبوت التعارض وانعدام الترجيح ويستدل على صحة هذا القول بما روي عن عمر أنه قال يوم السقيفة لأبي عبيدة ابسط يدك أبايعك فقال له أبو عبيدة أقول هذا وأبو بكر حاضر فعلم من رأيه أنه يراه أولى وأبو عبيدة أمين الأمة لا يخون والأمة تعتمد على قوله فبايع أبا بكر وانعقدت إمامته وانبرمت فدل هذا على أنها تنعقد بعقد واحد وكذا استدلوا عليه بعقد أبي بكر لعمر واستخلافه له من غير استعانة بغيره وجوزت الأمة ذلك وبايعوا عمر فكان هذا دليلا أن الإمامة تنعقد بعقد واحد بإجماع المسلمين والله الموفق قال بذلك أيضا الجويني في الإرشاد إلا أنه أضاف قال بعض أصحابنا لابد من جريان العقد بمشهد من الشهود فإنه لو لم يشترط ذلك لم نأمن أن يدع مدع عقدا سرا متقدما على الحق المظهر المبطن وليست الإمامة أحط رتبة من النكاح وقد شرط فيه الإعلان
The manner by which the imam is chosen: Then if the establishment of the caliphate by text is invalid, it is established that it is established by choice. Then people have speech regarding the manner of that, and I have not come across a school of our teachers (may Allah have mercy on them) on it. It is narrated from al-Ash'ari that if one of the people of ijtihad exercised ijtihad at the time of the death of an imam and saw the causes of suitability in someone established, and then contracted the imamah for him, it is contracted for him, and no one else after that may oppose him. There is no aspect to stipulating consensus today because of the delay of the imamah from the time of need for it if consensus were stipulated in it, especially since the Companions did not stipulate consensus in it at the time of selection and allegiance, but rather they considered the existence of the contract, then they obligated the allegiance after that. Then consensus, when it ceases to be a condition, the number of the few is not superior to the number of the many, so its consideration falls due to the establishment of conflict and lack of preponderance. One argues for the validity of this statement by what is narrated from 'Umar that he said on the day of Saqifa to Abu 'Ubaydah: 'Extend your hand so that I may pay allegiance to you.' Abu 'Ubaydah said to him: 'Do I say this while Abu Bakr is present?' So it was known from his opinion that he considered him more worthy. Abu 'Ubaydah is the trustee of the ummah, he does not betray, and the ummah relies on his statement. So he paid allegiance to Abu Bakr, and his imamah was contracted and concluded. This indicates that it is contracted by a single contract. Similarly, they argued by the contract of Abu Bakr for 'Umar and his appointing him as successor without seeking help from others, and the ummah permitted that and they paid allegiance to 'Umar. So this was evidence that the imamah is contracted by a single contract by consensus of the Muslims. And Allah is the grantor of success. Al-Juwayni also said that in al-Irshad, except that he added: Some of our companions said: It is necessary for the contract to occur in the presence of witnesses, because if that is not stipulated, we are not safe from someone claiming a secret contract prior to the manifest hidden truth, and the imamah is not lower in rank than marriage, and publicity has been stipulated in it.
Source pages p. 1133

Commentary
This passage is a proof and argument about the method of establishing the imamah. It begins by stating that since the imamah is not established by explicit text (nass), it is established by choice (ikhtiyar). Then al-Nasafi reports the view of al-Ash'ari that a single qualified person (ahl al-ijtihad) can contract the imamah for a suitable candidate, and once contracted, no one may oppose it. The passage then argues against requiring consensus (ijma') for the contract, because it would cause harmful delay; the Companions themselves did not require consensus at the selection of Abu Bakr, but only required the existence of the contract and then called for subsequent allegiance. Furthermore, if consensus is not a condition, then numbers do not matter, and no preference can be given to a larger group over a smaller one. To support this, al-Nasafi cites the event of Saqifa where 'Umar offered to pledge allegiance to Abu 'Ubaydah, but Abu 'Ubaydah deferred to Abu Bakr, and then 'Umar himself pledged to Abu Bakr, contracting the imamah. This shows a single contract suffices. A second example is Abu Bakr's appointment of 'Umar as his successor without consulting others, which the ummah accepted. The passage concludes with al-Juwayni's addition that the contract should be witnessed to prevent secret claims, drawing an analogy to marriage which requires publicity.

Key Terms
imamah
ikhtiyar
ijma'
'aqd
istikhlaf

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Sunni political theory requires universal consensus for the imam's appointment.
Correction: This passage shows that the contract of a single qualified person is sufficient, as evidenced by the Companions' actions.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the essential element for the imamah to be contracted?
A) Unanimous consensus of all Muslims
B) The contract of a single qualified person
C) A direct text from the previous imam
D) The allegiance of the majority of scholars

Key Takeaway
The imamah is validly contracted by a single qualified person through choice, not requiring universal consensus, as evidenced by the practices of the Companions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage shows that a single qualified person's decision was enough to contract the imāmah, and the community accepted it. Is there a leader in your life—a teacher, parent, or guide—whose judgment you trust so completely that you would follow their choice without needing everyone else's approval? What holds you back from that level of trust?
?
Al-Nasafī argues that requiring consensus would delay the imāmah beyond the time of need. When have you delayed an important decision because you sought everyone's agreement, and what was the cost of that delay in your own life?
?
The Companions accepted Abū Bakr's appointment of 'Umar without objection, even though it was a unilateral decision. Are there matters in your community or family where you resist a decision because it wasn't made by what you consider a 'fair' process? Does your resistance stem from a genuine concern or from pride?

The Qualifications and Imamate of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq
Al-Nasafi establishes Abu Bakr's caliphate by listing his qualifications and citing the consensus of the Companions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Nasafi establishes the rightful caliphate of Abu Bakr by listing his qualifications and citing the Companions' consensus.

Core Idea
Abu Bakr's caliphate is proven by his qualifications and the Companions' agreement.

Imam al-Nasafi has already laid out the general conditions for imamah. Now he applies them to Abu Bakr, showing that Abu Bakr possessed all the necessary qualities. Moreover, the fact that the Companions chose him and that his leadership resolved disputes serves as further proof. This passage is crucial for understanding the Sunni doctrine of the caliphate.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recall that the obligation and conditions of imamah have been established earlier. This passage applies them to Abu Bakr.
2
Identify Abu Bakr's qualifications
Look at the list of qualities: Qurashite lineage, knowledge, piety, firmness, composure, military expertise, public policy.
Note that the passage emphasizes both religious and administrative qualities.
3
Notice the Companions' consensus
The passage states that the Companions chose him and obeyed him, which indicates their agreement.
This consensus is a key argument for his legitimacy.
4
Reflect on the argument from dispute resolution
Even if no other merit, his ability to remove disagreement among the Companions is sufficient evidence of his truthfulness.
This shows the wisdom of his appointment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في إمامة أبي بكر وإذا ثبت بما تقدم من وجوب الإمامة والشرائط التي من اتصف بها صلح للإمامة نقول بعد ذلك إن أبا بكر كان بعد رسول الله خليفة محقا وإماما حقا مفترض الطاعة واجب الاتباع فيما يورده ويصدره ويأمر به وينهى عنه الصديق مستجمع لشرائط الخلافة بعد الرسول وذلك لأنه استجمع شرائط صحة الخلافة فإنه كان قرشيا لا ريبة لأحد فيه وكان من وراء ذلك من العلم والديانة والصلابة ورباطة الجأش والعلم بتدابير الحروب والقيام بتهيئة الجيوش وتنفيذ السرايا ومعرفة سياسة العامة وتسوية أمور الرعية كله كان ثابتا ولهذا اختارته الصحابة وانقادوا لأوامره على ما نبين ولو لم يكن من بركة أيامه ويُمن تعينه إلا الارتفاع ما وقع من الاختلاف بين الصحابة لكان ذلك دليلا كافيا على كونه محقا فيما تولى هاديا مهديا فيما يفعل ويأمر
The discussion on the imamate of Abu Bakr. And if it is established by what has preceded regarding the obligation of the imamah and the conditions by which one who is described with them is fit for the imamah, we say after that: Indeed, Abu Bakr was after the Messenger of Allah a rightful caliph and a true imam, obedience to whom is obligatory, and following him is required in what he presents and issues and commands and forbids. Al-Siddiq possessed all the conditions of the caliphate after the Messenger. That is because he possessed all the conditions of the validity of the caliphate. He was a Qurashite, concerning whom no one had any doubt. Beyond that, he possessed knowledge, piety, firmness, composure, knowledge of military strategies, undertaking the preparation of armies, execution of expeditions, knowledge of public policy, and management of the subjects' affairs. All of this was established in him. For this reason the Companions chose him and submitted to his orders as we will explain. And had there been nothing from the blessing of his days and the auspiciousness of his appointment except the removal of the disagreement that occurred among the Companions, that would have been sufficient evidence that he was truthful in what he undertook, rightly guided and guiding in what he did and commanded.
Source pages p. 1134

Commentary
This passage is a declaration and proof of Abu Bakr's rightful caliphate. It begins by stating the general conditions for imamah established earlier, then asserts that Abu Bakr met all of them. The passage enumerates his qualities: he was a Qurashite with no doubt about his lineage; he possessed knowledge, piety, firmness, composure, military expertise, ability to organize armies and expeditions, political acumen, and administrative skill. Because of these, the Companions chose him and obeyed him. The passage then offers a powerful argument: even if Abu Bakr had no other merit than resolving the disputes that arose among the Companions after the Prophet's death, that alone would be sufficient evidence of the truth of his imamate and his guidance as a leader.

Key Terms
khilafa
imam
shara'it
Qurash
'ilm
diyana
salaba

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Abu Bakr's caliphate was merely political and did not have religious significance.
Correction: The passage emphasizes his divine guidance (hadiyan mahdiyan), showing that his leadership was both spiritual and temporal.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT mentioned as a quality of Abu Bakr in this passage?
A) Knowledge of military strategies
B) Eloquence in poetry
C) Piety and firmness
D) Qurashite lineage

Key Takeaway
Abu Bakr's caliphate is validated by his personal qualifications, the consensus of the Companions, and the divine blessing of his leadership resolving disputes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you had been among the Companions, would you have recognized Abu Bakr's leadership immediately, or would you have hesitated? What does your answer reveal about your own willingness to follow qualified leaders appointed by consensus?
?
The passage says Abu Bakr's removal of disagreement among the Companions is a blessing. Think of a specific disagreement in your life. Did you actively work to resolve it, or did you let it fester? How can you emulate Abu Bakr's unifying leadership starting today?
?
Abu Bakr possessed knowledge, piety, firmness, and political skill. Which of these qualities do you lack most? What one concrete step will you take this week to cultivate that quality in your own role, whether at home, work, or in your community?
