The First Dispute: The Prophet's Death and Abu Bakr's Response
Al-Nasafi recounts the first disagreement about the Prophet's death and Abu Bakr's role in settling it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of the first dispute after the Prophet's death and how Abu Bakr's response established a principle of relating events to Allah's eternity.

Core Idea
The first dispute was about the reality of death, not succession.

This historical passage from al-Nasafi describes the very first disagreement that arose among the Muslims after the Prophet's death. It highlights 'Umar's initial denial and Abu Bakr's firm confirmation through the Quran and a powerful sermon. This event set a crucial precedent for dealing with religious crises by returning to divine revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the sequence of events.
Notice that the passage cuts off mid-sentence; we only have the beginning of the burial dispute.
2
Identify the two disputes
The passage mentions two disputes: first about whether the Prophet died, and second about where to bury him. Focus on the first dispute and how it was resolved.
3
Reflect on Abu Bakr's sermon
Consider the key phrase: 'Whoever worshipped Muhammad, then Muhammad has died; whoever worshipped the Lord of Muhammad, then the Lord of Muhammad is alive and does not die.' What does this teach about the relationship between created beings and the Creator?
This distinction is a core principle in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خلافات وقعت بعد وفاة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم انتهت برأي أبي بكر فإن أول خلاف وقع بين الأمة كان الخلاف في وفاة النبي فإن عمر كان يقول إنه عليه السلام لم يمت وإن من قال إنه مات أفعل به كذا وكذا إلى أن أتى أبو بكر الصديق وأخبر أنه مات وتلا قوله تعالى {إنك ميت وإنهم ميتون} ثم صعد للمنبر وخطب وقال في خطبته ألا من كان يعبد محمدا فإن محمدا قد مات ومن كان يعبد رب محمد فإن رب محمد حي لا يموت فاجتمع الناس على موته ثم اختلفوا في موضع دفنه فأشار المهاجرون إلى أن يدفن بمكة لأنها
Disputes that occurred after the death of the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, which ended with the opinion of Abu Bakr. For the first dispute that occurred among the ummah was the dispute regarding the death of the Prophet. 'Umar was saying that he, peace be upon him, had not died, and that whoever said he had died, he would do such and such to him, until Abu Bakr al-Siddiq came and informed that he had died, and recited the words of Allah Almighty: {Indeed, you are to die, and indeed, they are to die} [Qur'an 39:30]. Then he ascended the pulpit and delivered a sermon, saying in his sermon: 'Behold, whoever worshipped Muhammad, then Muhammad has died; and whoever worshipped the Lord of Muhammad, then the Lord of Muhammad is alive and does not die.' So the people agreed upon his death. Then they disagreed about the place of his burial; the Emigrants suggested that he be buried in Mecca, because it is...
Source pages p. 1134

Commentary
This passage is a historical narration about the first disagreement after the Prophet's death. It begins by stating that the first dispute was about whether the Prophet had actually died. 'Umar initially refused to accept the death and threatened anyone who said so. Then Abu Bakr came, confirmed the death, and recited the Qur'anic verse from Surah Az-Zumar (39:30) which states that the Prophet will die. He then gave a sermon on the pulpit, emphasizing that worship belongs only to Allah, who is eternal. This united the people in accepting the Prophet's death. The passage then mentions a second dispute about where to bury the Prophet; the Emigrants (Muhajirun) suggested Mecca, but the text cuts off at the page end.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first dispute after the Prophet's death was about who would succeed him as caliph.
Correction: The passage clearly shows that the first dispute was about whether the Prophet had actually died, not about succession. The succession issue came later.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Abu Bakr's sermon forced the people to choose between worshipping a mortal messenger or the eternal Lord. In your daily actions and focus, which one do you actually worship? Be honest: where does your ultimate love and fear lie?

Abu Bakr's Rightful Caliphate and Qualifications
The author asserts and proves Abu Bakr's rightful caliphate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the deductive argument for Abu Bakr's caliphate and the specific qualifications that made him worthy of leadership according to classical Sunni theology.

Core Idea
Abu Bakr's caliphate was legitimated by his qualifications and the end of discord.

This passage presents a structured proof for Abu Bakr's (RA) rightful succession to the Prophet (PBUH). The author first establishes the general criteria for imamate, then demonstrates how Abu Bakr met each one, and finally points to the historical outcome—the resolution of disagreement among the Companions—as a further sign of divine approval.

Guided Path
1
Understand the deductive structure
Notice that the author first states a general rule (conditions of imamate) and then applies it to Abu Bakr. This is a common theological method.
2
List the qualifications
Identify each condition mentioned: Quraysh lineage, knowledge, piety, firmness, composure, military and political expertise.
These are not arbitrary but derived from Islamic principles of leadership.
3
Examine the Companions' choice
The author uses the consensus of the Companions (ijma) as evidence of Abu Bakr's worthiness. Reflect on why this is a strong proof in Sunni theology.
4
Consider the final argument
The author argues that even the removal of discord among the Companions is sufficient proof. Think about how historical harmony is used as evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في إمامة أبي بكر: وإذا ثبت بما تقدم من وجوب الإمامة والشرائط التي من اتصف بها صلح للإمامة نقول بعد ذلك: إن أبا بكر الله كان بعد رسول الله عليه السلام خليفة محقا وإماما حقا مفترض الطاعة واجب الاتباع فيما يورده ويصدره ويأمر به وينهى عنه. وذلك لأنه له الله استجمع شرائط صحة الخلافة، فإنه كان قرشيًا لا ريبة لأحد فيه، وكان من وراء ذلك من العلم والديانة والصلابة ورباطة الجأش، والعلم بتدابير الحروب، والقيام بتهيئة الجيوش، وتنفيذ السرايا ومعرفة سياسة العامة، وتسوية أمور الرعية، كله كان ثابتا ولهذا اختارته الصحابة، وانقادوا لأوامره على ما نبين، ولو لم يكن من بركة أيامه ويُمن تعينه إلا الارتفاع ما وقع من الاختلاف بين الصحابة لكان ذلك دليلاً كافيًا على كونه محقا فيما تولى هاديًا مهديًا فيما يفعل ويأمر.
Discussion on the Imamate of Abu Bakr: Since it has been established by what preceded of the obligation of the imamate and the conditions by which whoever is described with them is fit for the imamate, we say after that: Indeed, Abu Bakr (may Allah be pleased with him) was after the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) a rightful caliph and a true imam whose obedience is obligatory and whose following is necessary in what he initiates, issues, commands, and forbids. This is because he (may Allah be pleased with him) gathered the conditions for the validity of the caliphate: he was a Qurashi with no doubt about him in that, and beyond that he possessed knowledge, piety, firmness, composure, knowledge of war strategies, undertaking the preparation of armies, dispatching expeditions, knowledge of public policy, and management of the subjects' affairs—all of this was established. For this reason the Companions chose him and submitted to his commands as we will explain. And if there were nothing from the blessings of his days and the auspiciousness of his appointment except the removal of the disagreement that occurred among the Companions, that would be sufficient evidence that he was rightful in what he undertook, a guide and rightly guided in what he does and commands.
Source pages p. 1134

Commentary
This passage is a deductive argument for the legitimacy of Abu Bakr's caliphate. The author begins by stating the general rule: the necessity of the imamate and the conditions that qualify a person for it. Then he applies this to Abu Bakr, asserting that he possessed all the required conditions. The conditions are enumerated: lineage (Qurashi), knowledge, piety, firmness, composure, military expertise (knowledge of war, armies, expeditions), political acumen (public policy, managing subjects). The author then notes that the Companions chose him and obeyed him. Finally, he argues that even if there were no other proof, the fact that Abu Bakr's caliphate ended the disputes among the Companions is itself sufficient evidence of his rightness. This argument uses both textual and historical reasoning.

Key Terms
إمامة
خليفة
شرائط الخلافة
قرشي
صحابة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author consider sufficient evidence for Abu Bakr's rightful caliphate beyond his possession of qualifications?
A) His being the first to accept Islam
B) The removal of disagreement among the Companions through his appointment
C) The recommendation of the Prophet
D) His military victories

Key Takeaway
Abu Bakr's caliphate is established through his fulfillment of leadership criteria, the consensus of the Companions, and the divinely ordained resolution of conflict.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument for Abu Bakr's caliphate includes the removal of disagreement among the Companions. Think of a conflict you are part of or aware of. Are you working to remove it, or are you adding to it? What would it cost you to be a peacemaker like Abu Bakr's leadership brought peace?
?
Abu Bakr is described as having knowledge, piety, firmness, composure, and ability to manage affairs. Which of these qualities do you most lack? What one concrete step will you take this week to cultivate that quality, so that you can be a source of unity rather than division?
?
The Companions submitted to Abu Bakr's command because they recognized his qualifications. Is there a righteous person in your life (a scholar, a parent, a leader) whose guidance you resist out of pride? How can you humbly submit, as the Companions did, for the sake of collective good?

The First Disagreement: The Prophet's Death and Abu Bakr's Resolution
The author recounts the dispute about the Prophet's death and how Abu Bakr resolved it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu Bakr resolved the first major disagreement among the early Muslims after the Prophet's death by affirming his mortality through Quranic recitation and a decisive sermon.

Core Idea
The Prophet's death is real; worship belongs only to the living Lord.

Immediately after the Prophet Muhammad's death, a profound shock gripped the community, leading Umar to deny that he had died. This passage from al-Nasafi recounts how Abu Bakr, through calm authority, Quranic proof, and a memorable sermon, restored unity and clarified the correct Islamic stance: the Prophet was mortal, but Allah alone is eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the narrative
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the sequence of events and the arguments used.
Pay attention to the emotional intensity of Umar's threat.
2
Identify the key figures and their positions
Notice Umar's initial denial and Abu Bakr's response; understand what each represents in terms of faith and leadership.
3
Reflect on the sermon's core message
Why did Abu Bakr say 'whoever worships Muhammad, Muhammad died; whoever worships Allah, Allah is alive'? This is the theological hinge of the passage.
Consider how this corrects any confusion between devotion to the Prophet and worship of Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أول خلاف وقع بين الأمة كان الخلاف في وفاة النبي عليه السلام، فإن عمر كان يقول: إنه عليه السلام لم يمت وإن من قال إنه مات أفعل به كذا وكذا، إلى أن أتى أبو بكر الصديق وأخبر أنه مات وتلا قوله تعالى: {إِنَّكَ مَيِّتٌ وَإِنَّهُم مَّيِّتُونَ} ثم صعد للمنبر وخطب وقال في خطبته: ألا من كان يعبد محمدًا فإن محمدًا قد مات ومن كان يعبد رب محمد فإن رب محمد حي لا يموت فاجتمع الناس على موته عليه السلام.
Indeed the first disagreement that occurred among the nation was the disagreement about the death of the Prophet (peace be upon him). For Umar was saying: He (peace be upon him) has not died, and whoever says he died, I will do such-and-such to him, until Abu Bakr al-Siddiq came and informed him that he had died and recited the saying of Allah Almighty: 'Indeed you are mortal, and they are mortal' (Surah az-Zumar:30). Then he ascended the pulpit and delivered a sermon, saying in his sermon: 'Behold, whoever worships Muhammad, indeed Muhammad has died; and whoever worships the Lord of Muhammad, indeed the Lord of Muhammad is alive and does not die.' So the people concurred on his death (peace be upon him).
Source pages p. 1134

Commentary
This passage narrates the first conflict after the Prophet's death: disbelief that he died, especially from Umar. Abu Bakr intervened, confirmed the death by reciting a Quranic verse, and then gave a famous sermon correcting the perspective. The key point is that his action united the community on the reality of the Prophet's death.

Key Terms
خلاف
وفاة النبي
أبو بكر الصديق
عمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Umar believed the Prophet had not died and would not die.
Correction: Abu Bakr corrected this by showing that the Prophet is mortal as the Quran states, and worship is due only to Allah.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Abu Bakr resolved the first Muslim dispute by affirming the Prophet's death through Quranic proof and redirecting worship to the eternal Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Who or what do you truly worship in your daily life? Is it the living Allah, or something that will one day die or fade away? What would it take for you to redirect all your devotion to the Eternal Lord?
?

?
Abu Bakr's sermon made a clear distinction between worshipping a mortal and worshipping the eternal Lord. In your own heart, are there any attachments that amount to 'worship' of something other than Allah? Name one attachment you need to break today.

The Burial Place Dispute and Abu Bakr's Resolution
The author describes the burial dispute and its resolution by Abu Bakr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Companions resolved the dispute over the Prophet's burial place through Abu Bakr's narration of a prophetic hadith.

Core Idea
Prophets are buried where they die.

After the Prophet's death, the Companions faced a dispute over his burial place—each group had a valid preference. This passage shows how Abu Bakr's report of a prophetic saying provided the definitive answer, unifying the community. Let's see how the different opinions were presented and resolved.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage and Translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of the dispute and its resolution.
Pay attention to the three groups and their reasoning.
2
Identify the Three Opinions
Note the Muhajirun's preference for Makkah, the suggestion of Jerusalem by some, and the Ansar's preference for Madinah. Understand each group's rationale.
Each opinion is based on a different aspect of the Prophet's life.
3
Understand the Role of Abu Bakr's Hadith
Abu Bakr narrated that prophets are buried where they die. This settled the dispute because the Prophet died in Madinah. Reflect on why his narration was accepted.
This hadith is not found in standard collections but was accepted by consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اختلفوا في موضع دفنه فأشار المهاجرون إلى أن يدفن بمكة، لأنها منشؤه وبلد آبائه وبعضهم أشاروا إلى نقله إلى مقابر الأنبياء ببيت المقدس، والأنصار قالوا إنه يدفن بالمدينة التي هي دار هجرته ومكان ظهور ملته وانتشار دعوته إلى أن روى أبو بكر الصديق الله: 'الأنبياء يدفنون حيث يقبضون' فقبلوا روايته وارتفع الخلاف ببركته.
Then they disagreed about the location of his burial. The Muhajirun indicated that he should be buried in Makkah, because it was his place of origin and the land of his forefathers. Some indicated transferring him to the graves of the prophets in Jerusalem. The Ansar said he should be buried in Madinah, which is the land of his emigration, the place of the emergence of his religion, and the spread of his call. Until Abu Bakr al-Siddiq (may Allah be pleased with him) narrated: 'The prophets are buried where they die.' So they accepted his narration, and the disagreement ceased by his blessing.
Source pages p. 1134

Commentary
This passage recounts the second dispute about where to bury the Prophet. Three opinions: Makkah, Jerusalem, Madinah. Abu Bakr ended it by reporting a hadith that prophets are buried where they die, which implied burial in Madinah (where he died). The Companions accepted his narration, and the dispute was resolved.

Key Takeaway
Abu Bakr's hadith that prophets are buried where they died resolved the Companions' dispute and established the Prophet's burial in Madinah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Companions accepted Abu Bakr's hadith and immediately ended their dispute. What would that act of submission cost you?
?
Abu Bakr's narration was a blessing that resolved the conflict. Are you actively seeking the 'blessing' of prophetic knowledge to heal divisions around you, or do you add to the conflict by insisting on your own view? Commit to one specific step you will take this week to bring a prophetic teaching into a current disagreement.

How the Companions Agreed on Abu Bakr's Succession
The author recounts the dispute about who should succeed the Prophet and how consensus on Abu Bakr was reached.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Companions resolved the dispute over succession and the reasons they unanimously chose Abu Bakr.

Core Idea
The Companions united on Abu Bakr through prophetic indications and his unparalleled virtues.

This passage from Tabsirat al-Adilla takes us into the historical moment after the Prophet's death when the community faced the question of leadership. We see the different opinions—Ansar wanting a share, some favoring al-Abbas, others Ali—and then the remarkable consensus on Abu Bakr. The author explains how this unity came about through divine guidance, prophetic signs, and Abu Bakr's own extraordinary qualities. As you read, notice how the passage grounds this political decision in theological and moral reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the flow from disagreement to agreement.
2
Identify the initial positions
Find the three groups mentioned: the Ansar, those inclined to al-Abbas, and those who supported Ali. Understand what each proposed.
The Ansar wanted a leader from each group, while others had specific candidates.
3
Understand the reasons for consensus
Notice the three 'either because' phrases that give reasons: scriptural indications, delegation of prayer and Hajj, and divine gathering of opinions.
The first two are from revelation and Prophetic practice; the third is a providential explanation.
4
Examine the list of virtues
The passage lists many superlative qualities of Abu Bakr. Count them and reflect on how each relates to leadership.
They include knowledge, intellect, courage, generosity, piety, and more.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اختلفوا في أمر الخلافة فكان الأنصار يقولون: منا أمير ومنكم أمير، وبعضهم كانوا يميلون إلى العباس، وبعضهم يرون عليا الله أحق بذلك، ثم ارتفع الخلاف واتفقت الآراء واجتمعت على أبي بكر الله، إما لما علموا بإشارة الكتاب أو الأحاديث على ما نبين وإما استدلالاً منهم بتفويض النبي عليه السلام إقامة ما هو من أعظم أركان الدين وهو الصلاة إليه، وأمره إياه بأن يحج بالناس سنة تسع عند قعوده عليه السلام عن إقامته بنفسه لعارض شغل له، وأو بأن اللطيف الخبير جل ثناؤه وتباركت أسماؤه نظر لأمة حبيبه ومتبعي صفيه ونجيبه جمع آراءهم المختلفة وأهواءهم المشتتة على من هو أكثرهم فضلاً، وأعزهم علمًا، وأوفرهم عقلاً، وأصوبهم تدبيراً وأربطهم عند الملمات جاشا، وأشدهم على وعد الله تعالى بإظهار الدين على الأديان اتكالاً، وأيمنهم نقيبة، وأعودهم على افتاء الخلق وطبقات الرعايا نفعًا، وأظلفهم عن الفواحش نفسا، وأصونهم عن القبائح عرضا، وأشدهم لأمور الدين تعظيمًا، وأقدمهم إسلاما، وأجودهم كفا، وأسمحهم، يبذل ما
Then they disagreed about the matter of the caliphate. The Ansar were saying: 'An amir from us and an amir from you.' Some were inclined toward al-Abbas, and some held that 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) was more entitled to it. Then the disagreement was lifted and opinions agreed and united upon Abu Bakr (may Allah be pleased with him), either because they knew from an indication of the Book or the hadiths as we will explain, or by deducing from the Prophet's delegation to him of the establishment of that which is among the greatest pillars of religion—namely prayer—and his command to him to lead the people in Hajj in the year nine when he himself could not perform it due to an obstacle, or because the Gentle and All-Knowing, exalted be His praise and blessed be His names, looked after the nation of His beloved and the followers of His chosen and noble one, and gathered their differing opinions and scattered desires upon the one who was most virtuous among them, most abundant in knowledge, most complete in intellect, most correct in planning, most steadfast in heart at times of calamity, most reliant on Allah's promise to manifest the religion over all religions, most auspicious in disposition, most beneficial in issuing verdicts for people and classes of subjects, most pure in soul from obscenities, most protective of his honor from base actions, most reverent of religious matters, earliest in Islam, most generous in hand, and most liberal, giving what...
Source pages p. 1135

Commentary
This passage discusses the third major dispute: the succession after the Prophet. The Ansar wanted one leader from them and one from the Muhajirun. Some supported al-Abbas, some supported 'Ali. Ultimately, consensus fell on Abu Bakr. The author gives two possible reasons: either they knew from scriptural indications, or they deduced from the Prophet's actions (e.g., appointing Abu Bakr to lead prayers and Hajj). Then the author gives a long list of Abu Bakr's superlative qualities that Allah brought the community to agree upon. The list includes virtue, knowledge, intellect, planning, steadfastness, reliance, auspiciousness, benefit, purity, etc. The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating the discussion continues.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The passage presents the selection as divinely guided and based on clear indications from the Prophet and Abu Bakr's superior virtues, not mere politics.

The Consensus on Abu Bakr's Caliphate
Describes caliphate dispute and consensus on Abu Bakr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reasons the early Muslim community united on Abu Bakr as caliph, including prophetic indications and his personal virtues.

Core Idea
Consensus on Abu Bakr rested on prophetic delegation and his unmatched excellence.

After the Prophet's death, the Muslim community faced a critical decision: who would lead them? This passage recounts the initial disagreements—some favored the Ansar, others al-Abbas or Ali—and then shows how all eventually agreed on Abu Bakr. The author explains that this unity came through divine guidance, either via explicit scriptural indications or through inference from the Prophet's actions, and he lists many of Abu Bakr's virtues that made him the most deserving. As you read, notice how the text moves from disagreement to consensus, grounding the legitimacy of Abu Bakr's caliphate in both revealed signs and rational inference from prophetic precedent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the caliphate dispute
Read the Arabic passage from 'ثم اختلفوا' up to the start of 'وكذا اختلفوا'. Follow the translation to understand the flow: initial differences, then agreement, reasons for consensus, and the list of virtues.
The passage is dense; take your time with the list of virtues.
2
Identify the initial opinions
Notice the three groups mentioned: the Ansar proposing a shared leadership, some preferring al-Abbas, and some considering Ali more entitled. Then observe how the disagreement ended in unity on Abu Bakr.
This shows the process of consultation and eventual consensus.
3
Analyze the reasons for consensus
The passage gives two categories of reasons: direct knowledge from Quran/Hadith, and inference from the Prophet's delegation of prayer and Hajj to Abu Bakr. Reflect on why these actions indicated his suitability for leadership.
The delegation of prayer is particularly significant as it is a public, religious duty.
4
Reflect on the virtues listed
The author enumerates many virtues of Abu Bakr, such as knowledge, intellect, steadfastness, generosity, and early Islam. Consider how these qualities made him the most deserving in the eyes of the community.
This list is not exhaustive but highlights key traits.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اختلفوا في أمر الخلافة فكان الأنصار يقولون : منا أمير ومنكم أمير، وبعضهم كانوا يميلون إلى العباس، وبعضهم يرون عليا الله أحق بذلك، ثم ارتفع الخلاف واتفقت الآراء واجتمعت على أبي بكر الله، إما لما علموا بإشارة الكتاب أو الأحاديث على ما نبين وإما استدلالاً منهم بتفويض النبي عليه السلام إقامة ما هو من أعظم أركان الدين وهو الصلاة إليه، وأمره إياه بأن يحج بالناس سنة تسع عند قعوده عليه السلام عن إقامته بنفسه لعارض شغل له، وأو بأن اللطيف الخبير جل ثناؤه وتباركت أسماؤه نظر لأمة حبيبه ومتبعي صفيه ونجيبه جمع آراءهم المختلفة وأهواءهم المشتتة على من هو أكثرهم فضلاً، وأعزهم علمًا، وأوفرهم عقلاً، وأصوبهم تدبيراً وأربطهم عند الملمات جاشا، وأشدهم على وعد الله تعالى بإظهار الدين على الأديان اتكالاً، وأيمنهم نقيبة، وأعودهم على افتاء الخلق وطبقات الرعايا نفعًا، وأظلفهم عن الفواحش نفسا، وأصونهم عن القبائح عرضا، وأشدهم لأمور الدين تعظيمًا، وأقدمهم إسلاما، وأجودهم كفا، وأسمحهم، يبذل ما احتوى عليه من المال في ذات الله يدًا وأقلهم في ذات الله تعالى مبالاة عن لومة لائم وملاحاة جاهل - فرضوان الله عليه وتحياته وعلى متبعيه ومحبيه، ولعنة الله تعالى والملائكة والناس أجمعين على مبغضيه. وكذا اختلفوا في تنفيذ جيش أسامة بن زيد، وفي ترك الصدقات، في تلك السدة وأبى هو إلا تنفيذ ما أمر به رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وقال : لا أحل عقدة عقدها رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم - وقال في باب الصدقات: - والله لو منعوني عناقاً أو عقالاً مما كانوا يؤدون إلى رسول الله لقاتلتهم عليه - فانقادوا كلهم لأمره وساعدوه على رأيه.
Then they differed over the matter of the caliphate. The Helpers said: 'From us an amir and from you an amir.' Some of them inclined to al-Abbas, and some considered Ali to be more entitled to it. Then the disagreement ceased and opinions agreed and united upon Abu Bakr, either because they knew from an indication of the Book or the Hadith as we will explain, or by way of inference from the Prophet's delegation to him of the establishment of the greatest pillar of the religion, which is prayer, and his order to him to lead the people in Hajj in the year nine when he himself could not lead due to an impediment, or because the Subtle, the All-Aware—exalted is His praise and blessed are His names—looked after the community of His beloved and the followers of His chosen and intimate friend by gathering their differing opinions and scattered inclinations upon the one who was the most virtuous among them, the most knowledgeable, the most complete in intellect, the most correct in planning, the most steadfast in heart at calamities, the most reliant on God's promise to manifest the religion over all religions, the most blessed in disposition, the most beneficial to creation and the classes of subjects, the most chaste in soul from obscenities, the most protective of honor from vile deeds, the most reverent of religious matters, the earliest in Islam, the most generous in hand, the most open-handed, spending whatever wealth he possessed in the cause of God, and the least concerned in the cause of God Almighty about the blame of a blamer or the dispute of an ignorant person. May God's pleasure and salutations be upon him, and upon his followers and lovers, and may the curse of God, the angels, and all people be upon his haters. Likewise, they differed over the dispatch of the army of Usama ibn Zayd and over abandoning the alms. In that [courtyard] he refused except to carry out what the Messenger of God had commanded, saying: 'I will not untie a knot that the Messenger of God tied.' And regarding alms he said: 'By God, if they withhold from me a young she-goat or a rope that they used to pay to the Messenger of God, I would fight them for it.' So they all submitted to his command and assisted him in his opinion.
Source pages p. 1135

Commentary
This passage is a historical and theological account of the caliphate after the Prophet's death. It first describes the initial dispute: the Ansar proposed a ruler from them and one from the Muhajirun; some favored al-Abbas; some believed Ali was more entitled. Then the disagreement resolved, and everyone united on Abu Bakr. The author gives several reasons for this consensus: either through explicit indication from the Quran or Hadith, or through inference from the Prophet's delegation of leading prayer and Hajj to Abu Bakr, indicating his preeminence. The author then lists numerous virtues of Abu Bakr that made him the most deserving: his virtue, knowledge, intellect, correct planning, steadfastness, reliance on God, beneficial nature, moral purity, early Islam, generosity, and fearlessness in God's cause. The passage concludes with blessings on him and curses on his enemies. Then it mentions two further issues after Abu Bakr became caliph: the dispatch of Usama's army (which the Prophet had ordered) and the collection of alms from those who refused. Abu Bakr insisted on carrying out the Prophet's commands exactly, saying he would not untie any knot the Prophet tied, and he vowed to fight those who withheld alms. The community eventually submitted to his decision and supported him.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The caliphate was settled by force or political maneuvering without legitimate basis.
Correction: The passage shows the community initially had differing opinions but eventually reached consensus on Abu Bakr based on prophetic indications and his personal virtues, indicating a divinely guided process.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was one of the reasons the community agreed on Abu Bakr for caliph?
A) He was the wealthiest.
B) The Prophet delegated him to lead prayer.
C) He was the oldest.
D) He was from the Ansar.

Key Takeaway
The consensus on Abu Bakr's caliphate was not arbitrary but based on prophetic signs and his extraordinary virtues.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you had been among the Muslims at that time, which of the initial positions would you have been most inclined to support? What does that reveal about your own criteria for leadership?
?
The passage lists many virtues of Abu Bakr. Which of these virtues do you most lack in your own character, and what concrete step will you take this week to begin cultivating it?
?
Do you truly believe that divine wisdom guided the community to Abu Bakr, or do you harbor doubts that it was merely human politics? How would your life change if you fully internalized that God orchestrates events for His chosen ones?

Abu Bakr's Firm Actions as Caliph
Describes Abu Bakr's firm actions as caliph.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu Bakr's insistence on carrying out the Prophet's commands regarding Usama's army and zakat established the authority of his caliphate and unified the community.

Core Idea
True leadership means upholding the Prophet's commands without compromise.

After the consensus on his caliphate, Abu Bakr faced immediate challenges: some questioned the dispatch of Usama's army (as ordered by the Prophet) and some withheld alms. This passage shows how Abu Bakr remained firm, declaring he would not untie any knot tied by the Prophet and vowing to fight those who withheld what they used to pay to the Messenger. Remarkably, the community eventually submitted to his decision. As you read, notice how Abu Bakr's steadfastness in following the Prophet's directives solidified his leadership.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the two disputes
Read the Arabic from 'وكذا اختلفوا' to the end. Follow the translation: it covers the disagreement over Usama's army and over zakat, Abu Bakr's firm responses, and the community's submission.
Note the phrase 'في تلك السدة'—likely meaning 'in that situation' or 'courtyard'.
2
Analyze Abu Bakr's reasoning
Abu Bakr's statements: 'I will not untie a knot that the Messenger of God tied' and his threat to fight those who withhold alms. What do these reveal about his view of the caliphate's role?
He sees his role as implementing the Prophet's orders exactly.
3
Observe the outcome
The passage states 'فانقادوا كلهم لأمره وساعدوه على رأيه'—they all submitted and assisted him. This shows that firmness in truth can unite the community.

Quranic Proof for Abu Bakr's Caliphate from Surah al-Fath
Interprets verse 48:16 as indicating Abu Bakr's caliphate and obligating obedience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the interpretation of Surah al-Fath (48:16) provides a scriptural basis for the obligation to obey Abu Bakr as caliph.

Core Idea
Obedience to the caller in the Quran proves the caliph's authority.

This lesson examines a key Quranic proof used by Maturidi theologians to establish the legitimacy of Abu Bakr's caliphate. By analyzing verse 48:16, the author argues that the verse implies the caliph's obedience is obligatory. Let's see how this argument works and the early scholarly differences on who the 'people of severe might' were.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and its context
Start by reading the verse from Surah al-Fath (48:16) and its surrounding context to understand the situation of those addressed.
The verse addresses bedouins who lagged behind.
2
Understand the author's argument linking obedience to the caller
Notice how the author points to the conditional promise of reward and threat of punishment as a sign that the caller (the caliph) must be obeyed.
This is the core of the proof.
3
Reflect on the differing interpretations
Consider the two interpretations of 'people of severe might' (Banu Hanifa vs. Persians) and see that the argument does not depend on a specific identification.
This shows flexibility in the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الدليل من كتاب الله تعالى على ذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿ قُل لِّلْمُخَلَّفِينَ مِنَ الْأَعْرَابِ سَتُدْعَونَ إِلَى قَوْمٍ أُولِي بَأْسٍ شَدِيدٍ نُقَتِلُونَهُمْ أَوْ يُسْلِمُونَ ﴾ أمر الله تعالى نبيه عليه السلام أن يقول للذين تخلفوا عن الغزو معه أنكم ستدعون إلى قوم أولي بأس شديد، وأشار في الآية إلى أن الداعي مفترض الطاعة ينالون الثواب بطاعتهم إياه وإجابتهم إلى ما دعاهم إليه، ويستحقون التعذيب بالعذاب الأليم بعصيانهم إياه وإعراضهم عن الإجابة إلى ما دعاهم إليه، فإنه تعالى قال: ﴿ فَإِن تُطِيعُوا يُؤْتِكُمُ اللَّهُ أَجْرًا حَسَنًا وَإِن تَتَوَلَّوْا كَمَا تَوَلَّيْتُم مِّن قَبْلُ يُعَذِّبْكُمْ عَذَابًا أَلِيمًا ﴾ وهذا هو أمارة كون الداعي مفترض الطاعة، ثم سلف الأمة اختلفوا في المراد بقوله تعالى: أُولِي بَأْسٍ شَدِيدٍ منهم من قال: بنو حنيفة ومنهم من قال: هم أهل فارس على ما قال تعالى في آية أخرى ﴿ بعثنا عليْكُمْ عِبَادًا لَنَا أُولِي بَأْسٍ شَدِيدٍ ﴾ والمراد منه أهل فارس وهم جنود بخت نصر.
Then the proof from the Book of God Almighty for that is His statement: {Say to the bedouins who were left behind: 'You will be called to a people of severe might; you will fight them or they will submit.'} God Almighty commanded His Prophet to say to those who lagged behind from fighting with him: 'You will be called to a people of severe might.' And He indicated in the verse that the caller is one whose obedience is obligatory—they attain reward by obeying him and responding to what he calls them to, and they deserve painful punishment by disobeying him and turning away from responding to what he calls them to. For He Almighty said: {If you obey, God will give you a good reward; but if you turn away as you turned away before, He will punish you with a painful punishment.} And this is a sign that the caller is one whose obedience is obligatory. Then the predecessors of the community differed over the meaning of His statement 'people of severe might.' Some said: Banu Hanifa; some said: they are the people of Persia, as God said in another verse: {We sent against you servants of Ours, people of severe might.} And what is meant by that is the people of Persia, and they are the armies of Nebuchadnezzar.
Source pages p. 1136

Commentary
This passage provides a Quranic argument for the legitimacy of Abu Bakr's caliphate. The author cites verse 48:16 from Surah al-Fath, where God tells the Prophet to address those bedouins who stayed behind from the expedition: they will be called to fight a people of severe might. The author argues that the verse implies the caller (dā‘ī) is one whose obedience is obligatory (muftaraḍ al-ṭā‘ah), because God promises reward for obedience and threatens punishment for disobedience to that caller. This caller is identified as the caliph who summoned them to fight, namely Abu Bakr. The author notes that early scholars differed on the identity of the 'people of severe might': some said they were Banu Hanifa (the tribe of Musaylimah), others said they were the Persians (the armies of Nebuchadnezzar, based on another verse). The key point is that the verse establishes that the caliph who calls to jihad must be obeyed, and this obedience is divinely sanctioned. Thus, Abu Bakr's caliphate is supported by scripture.

Key Terms
داعي
مفترض الطاعة
أولي بأس شديد
بنو حنيفة
أهل فارس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse only applies to the Prophet's time and has no bearing on the caliphate.
Correction: The author argues that the verse applies to any caller who is divinely appointed, such as Abu Bakr, because the structure of the verse implies ongoing obligation.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what indicates that the caller in verse 48:16 is one whose obedience is obligatory?
A) The caller is explicitly named as Abu Bakr.
B) God promises reward for obedience and punishment for disobedience to the caller.
C) The people of severe might are identified as Banu Hanifa.
D) The verse was revealed in Medina.

Key Takeaway
The key takeaway is that verse 48:16 is interpreted to support the binding authority of Abu Bakr's caliphate through the sign of obligatory obedience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The verse promises reward for obeying the caller and punishment for turning away. Who is the 'caller' in your life that God expects you to obey without hesitation? Is your heart quick to submit or does it search for loopholes?
?
If you truly believed that disobedience to a legitimate leader brings a painful punishment from God, would your attitude toward leadership change? What would it mean for you to embrace that accountability today?
?
The author applies this Quranic principle to Abu Bakr, but the lesson remains: God grants authority and demands obedience. Are you willing to accept that God's command to obey a leader is not optional? What would have to change in your heart for you to submit fully?

Quranic Proof for the Caliphate of Abu Bakr and Umar
Proof from Quran for caliphate of Abu Bakr and Umar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a Quranic verse implicitly supports the caliphate of Abu Bakr and Umar through the logic of apostasy and thankfulness.

Core Idea
The verse about turning back proves Abu Bakr led the thankful against apostates.

This passage presents a sophisticated argument linking a Quranic verse about apostasy to the legitimacy of the first two caliphs. By examining who is meant by 'the thankful' and 'the turncoats,' the author shows that Abu Bakr led the steadfast believers, establishing his caliphate and, by extension, Umar's. Let's explore the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two interpretations
The author starts by considering two possible referents for a pronoun. Identify them and see how each leads to proving either Abu Bakr's or Umar's caliphate.
Pay attention to the logical 'if...then' structure.
2
Follow the logical chain
Notice how establishing one caliphate automatically establishes the other due to succession or appointment.
The argument relies on the mutual dependency of their caliphates.
3
Examine the Quranic verse
Read the verse cited (Al-Imran 3:144) and understand its context about turning back after the Prophet's death.
Consider how the verse distinguishes two groups.
4
Connect to al-Hasan's statement
See how al-Hasan al-Basri identifies Abu Bakr as the imam of the thankful, linking to the verse.
Al-Hasan's comment is crucial for the reasoning.
5
Confirm the historical context
The mention of apostasy only during Abu Bakr's era cements the interpretation.
The passage explicitly states that no apostasy occurred outside Abu Bakr's caliphate.

فإن كان المراد منه بني حنيفة فقد كان الداعي إليهم أبا بكر انه فثبتت بذلك خلافته، وإذا ثبتت خلافته ثبتت خلافة من عقد هو له الخلافة واستخلفه بعده وهو عمر الله، وإن كان المراد منه أهل فارس فالداعي إليهم كان عمر بالآية خلافته، وبثبوت خلافته ثبتت خلافة من كان هو خليفة له عقد هو له وهو أبو بكر الله، فكان في الآية دليل خلافة الشيخين، والدليل عليه قوله تعالى: أَفَإِيْن مَاتَ أَوْ قُتِلَ انقَلَبْتُمْ عَلَى أَعْقَبِكُمْ وَمَن يَنقَلِبْ عَلَى عَقِبَيْهِ فَلَن يَضُرَّ اللَّهَ شَيْئًا وَسَيَجْزِي اللَّهُ الشَّاكِرِينَ فأخبر عز وجل أن فيهم من ينقلب على عقبيه وفيهم من يشكر الله بالصبر على دينه والقيام به وقال الحسن: كان أبو بكر الله إمام الشاكرين، لأن الذين صبروا على دين الله هم الذين حاربوا المنقلبين فكان هو إمامهم، وكان ذكر الانقلاب من الله تعالى عند موت نبيه عليه السلام، فثبت أنه كان في عهد أبي بكر إذ لا ارتداد كان إلا في عهده، ولا خرج إلى أهل الردة إلا أصحاب أبي بكر الله وعنهم فكانوا شاكرين وأبو بكر إمامهم، والله الموفق.
If what is meant by it is Banu Hanifa, then the caller to them was Abu Bakr, so his caliphate is established by that. And if his caliphate is established, the caliphate of the one for whom he arranged the caliphate and appointed as his successor after him, namely Umar, is established. And if what is meant by it is the people of Persia, then the caller to them was Umar, and his caliphate is established by the verse [?]. And by the establishment of his caliphate, the caliphate of the one for whom he was a caliph, for whom he arranged it, namely Abu Bakr, is established. So in the verse there is evidence for the caliphate of the two Shaykhs. The evidence for it is the saying of Allah Most High: 'Then if he dies or is killed, will you turn back on your heels? And whoever turns back on his heels will never harm Allah at all, and Allah will reward the thankful.' So He, Mighty and Majestic, informed that among them are those who turn back on their heels and among them are those who thank Allah by being patient upon His religion and upholding it. And al-Hasan said: Abu Bakr was the imam of the thankful, because those who were patient upon the religion of Allah are those who fought the turncoats, so he was their imam. And the mention of turning back from Allah Most High came at the time of the death of His Prophet, peace be upon him. So it is established that it was in the era of Abu Bakr, since there was no apostasy except in his era, and none went out to fight the people of apostasy except the companions of Abu Bakr and from them [?], so they were thankful and Abu Bakr was their imam. And Allah is the Giver of success.
Source pages p. 1137

Commentary
This passage is a proof from the Quran for the legitimacy of the caliphates of Abu Bakr and Umar (may Allah be pleased with them). The author begins by considering two possible interpretations of the pronoun 'them' in a prior context (likely referring to groups mentioned earlier). If 'them' refers to Banu Hanifa (the tribe of Musaylimah the liar), then the one who called them to Islam and fought them was Abu Bakr, thereby proving his caliphate. Since Abu Bakr appointed Umar as his successor, Umar's caliphate is also proven. If 'them' refers to the people of Persia, then the caller to them was Umar, proving his caliphate, and by extension Abu Bakr's, since Abu Bakr had appointed Umar. Thus the verse contains evidence for both caliphates. The author then cites the Quranic verse (Al-Imran 3:144) which speaks of those who turn back on their heels and those who are thankful. Allah informs that after the Prophet's death, some would apostatize and some would remain steadfast. Al-Hasan al-Basri is quoted saying that Abu Bakr was the imam of the thankful, because the thankful ones are those who patiently adhered to the religion and fought the apostates, and Abu Bakr led them. The mention of turning back at the time of the Prophet's death proves that this occurred in Abu Bakr's era, since apostasy happened only during his caliphate, and only his companions fought the apostates. Thus the thankful ones are Abu Bakr's companions, and he is their imam. The proof concludes that this verse supports the caliphate of the first two caliphs.

Key Terms

الشاكرين
المنقلبين
ارتداد
إمام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse about turning back only refers to the Prophet's lifetime and has no political implications.
Correction: The author shows that the verse explicitly mentions events after the Prophet's death, namely apostasy, and al-Hasan identifies Abu Bakr as leading the thankful who fought apostates, thus establishing a proof for his caliphate.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the mention of 'turning back on your heels' at the time of the Prophet's death imply?
A) That apostasy would occur in the time of Umar
B) That Abu Bakr was the leader of the apostates
C) That apostasy occurred during Abu Bakr's caliphate
D) That there would be no apostasy

Key Takeaway
The verse about turning back demonstrates that Abu Bakr led the steadfast believers against the apostates, thereby proving his caliphate and, by extension, Umar's.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'will you turn back on your heels?' do you feel a personal accountability to remain steadfast, or do you assume it only applies to others? What would it mean for you to be among the 'thankful' in this specific sense of actively fighting against apostasy—what are the 'apostasies' in your own life that require you to fight?
?
The author proves that Abu Bakr was the imam of the thankful by his actions. If your actions were the only evidence of your gratitude to Allah, would they suffice to prove that you are among the thankful? What one concrete action can you take today to demonstrate that thankfulness in a way that is undeniable?
?
This lesson ties the legitimacy of leadership to a specific historical event—the Ridda wars. Does your understanding of who leads the Muslim community today rest on similar concrete evidence, or on convenience? How can you ensure that your allegiance to leaders is rooted in their defense of the religion, not in personal benefit?

Consensus as a Decisive Proof of Caliphate
Definition of consensus as a decisive proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the consensus of the Companions is considered a decisive proof for the caliphate of Abu Bakr, equal in certainty to the Quran and mutawatir hadith.

Core Idea
Consensus is a definitive proof, as binding as the Quran and Sunnah.

In defending the caliphate of Abu Bakr, the author first establishes the methodological foundation: the consensus of the Companions is not merely a historical agreement but a source of certain knowledge. This passage explains why ijma' carries such weight in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice the emphatic language describing consensus.
2
Identify the claim about consensus
The passage equates consensus with the Quran and prophetic reports in terms of certainty. Identify the key phrase that expresses this.
Look for 'حجة قاطعة' and 'موجبة للعلم قطعًا'.
3
Reflect on the importance
Why would the author insist on consensus being a certain proof? Think about the context of defending a political decision.
This connects to the broader argument for Abu Bakr's legitimacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على صحة خلافته إجماع الصحابة عليها، والإجماع حجة قاطعة موجبة للعلم قطعًا كنص كتاب الله تعالى وخبر الرسول عليه السلام المسموع منه أو الآيات الثابتة عنه بطريق لا يتوهم فيه شبهة الانقطاع. اعتراض، والرد عليه: فإن قالوا: دعوى الإجماع ممنوعة فإن عليًا تأخر عن بيعته ستة أشهر أو أربعة أشهر. قلنا: وقد انعقد بعد بيعته وبه يحتج فلم يكن في هذا السؤال لهم منفعة، ثم تأخره محمول على الاشتغال بالنظر ليتضح له وجوه الصواب فيتابع عامة الصحابة ويبايعه، أو يبدو له وجه خطئهم فيعلن مخالفتهم، ويجاهر بالمكاشفة ويشهر عليهم سيفه، كما يليق بكمال علمه وقوة ديانته ورباطة جأشه، ولذا لم يظهر في تلك المدة من المنازعة، فلما لاح له بعد طول التردي وإدمان النظر وجه الصواب وافق غيره من الصحابة وبايع الصديق اتباعًا للحق كما هو اللائق بحاله في جلالة قدره وعظم شأنه، لا خوفا على نفسه وأهاليه وتوقيًا على مكروه يناله في نفسه كما بهم الروافض، إذ هو بعيد عن حاله ممتنع منه على ما تقدم من ذكره في بيان شهامته وصرامته ومشهور نجدته وشجاعته ومخبور غنائه وبسالته، وما عرف من صلابته في ديانته وقوة عشيرته وعزة قبيلته. الإجماع حجة ملزمة كالنص: ومن أعجب الأشياء دعوى الروافض أن عليًا بايع أبا بكر تقية مع ما يدعون من وصف أبي بكر بالضعف والجبن ووصف علي بالقوة وغاية الشجاعة والله الموفق.
And the evidence for the validity of his caliphate is the consensus of the Companions upon it. And consensus is a decisive proof, necessarily producing certain knowledge, like the text of the Book of Allah Most High and the report of the Messenger, peace be upon him, heard from him or the verses established from him in a manner in which no suspicion of discontinuity is conceivable. Objection and response to it: If they say: The claim of consensus is rejected because Ali delayed his pledge for six months or four months. We say: The consensus was concluded after his pledge, and it is used as evidence, so there is no benefit for them in this question. Moreover, his delay is interpreted as preoccupation with deliberation to clarify for himself the aspects of correctness, so that he might follow the generality of the Companions and pledge allegiance, or it might become apparent to him their error, so he would announce his opposition, openly declare it, and draw his sword against them, as befits his complete knowledge, the strength of his religiosity, and his firmness of heart. Therefore, no dispute appeared during that period. When, after prolonged hesitation and deep reflection, the correct course became clear to him, he agreed with the other Companions and pledged allegiance to al-Siddiq, following the truth, as befits his state in the majesty of his rank and the greatness of his stature—not out of fear for himself and his family nor to avoid harm that might befall him, as the Rafidah claim. For such is far from his state and impossible for him, given what has preceded of mention of his courage, sharpness, renowned valor, bravery, tested sufficiency, and prowess, and what is known of his firmness in his religion, the strength of his clan, and the honor of his tribe. Consensus is a binding proof like the text: And among the most astonishing things is the claim of the Rafidah that Ali pledged allegiance to Abu Bakr out of taqiyyah, despite their claim that they describe Abu Bakr as weak and cowardly and describe Ali as possessing strength and utmost courage. And Allah is the Giver of success.
Source pages p. 1137

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the consensus (ijma') of the Companions on Abu Bakr's caliphate. The author first states that consensus is a decisive proof, equivalent to the Quran and mutawatir hadith. Then an objection is raised: the claim of consensus is invalid because Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) delayed his pledge for six or four months. The response begins by noting that consensus was established after Ali eventually pledged, so the objection does not negate the consensus. The delay is then explained as stemming from Ali's careful deliberation: he wanted to examine the matter thoroughly to either join the majority if they were correct or openly oppose and fight if they were wrong. This is consistent with his knowledge, religiosity, and courage. The fact that no conflict erupted during that period shows he was not in opposition. Once the truth became clear after prolonged reflection, he agreed with the others and pledged, following the truth—not out of fear or taqiyyah, as the Rafidah (extreme Shiites) claim. The author argues that such taqiyyah is impossible for Ali given his well-known courage, strength, and familial power. The passage concludes by calling the Rafidah claim astonishing, as it contradicts their own characterization of Abu Bakr as weak and Ali as strong. The section ends with a reaffirmation that consensus is a binding proof.

Key Terms
إجماع
حجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, consensus is a proof that produces what kind of knowledge?
A) Probable knowledge
B) Certain knowledge
C) Speculative knowledge
D) Historical knowledge

Key Takeaway
The consensus of the Companions on Abu Bakr's caliphate is a certain proof, equivalent to the Quran and mutawatir hadith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you disagree with the consensus of the righteous predecessors, do you realize you are rejecting a source of certainty equivalent to the Quran? How can you humble yourself before that certainty today?
?
Is there any matter—big or small—where you have set aside the clear consensus of the Companions? What will you do to realign your heart with this truth?
?
If the Companions' consensus on Abu Bakr's caliphate is a decisive proof, then rejecting it is akin to rejecting the Qur'an. Have you ever considered this? How does that challenge your own stance on this issue?

Responding to the Objection of Ali's Delayed Pledge
Refutation of the objection regarding Ali's delayed pledge and the claim of taqiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that Ali's delayed pledge invalidates the consensus on Abu Bakr's caliphate, and why the claim of taqiyya is rejected.

Core Idea
Ali's delay was due to careful deliberation, not rejection; he was too courageous to pledge out of fear.

Having established consensus as proof, the author now addresses a specific historical objection: Ali's delay in pledging. The response not only defends the consensus but also refutes the sectarian claim that Ali acted under duress, using Ali's own known character as evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start by reading the objection raised by the opponents. Note the specific claim about Ali's delay.
The objection is that the consensus is invalid because Ali did not immediately pledge.
2
Follow the response
The response first argues that the consensus was concluded after Ali's pledge, then explains the reason for the delay. Pay attention to the shift in reasoning.
3
Examine the refutation of taqiyya
The author uses Ali's known courage to argue that he would not pledge under fear. How does this strengthen the case?
This is a key refutation of the Rafidah claim.
4
Synthesize the argument
Combine the parts: the consensus is intact, and the delay was a sign of careful thought, not opposition.

Refutation of the objection about Ali's delayed bay'ah
The author refutes the objection that Ali's delayed bay'ah undermines consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi scholar refutes the objection that Ali's delayed pledge undermines the legitimacy of Abu Bakr's caliphate, demonstrating that consensus was still achieved and that Ali's behavior was consistent with his noble character.

Core Idea
Ali's delay in pledging was thoughtful reflection, not rejection of consensus.

The objection that Ali's delayed bay'ah invalidates consensus is a common sectarian argument. In this passage, the author responds by explaining that Ali eventually pledged, and his delay was for careful deliberation to determine the truth. He also refutes the claim that Ali acted out of fear, highlighting Ali's renowned bravery. This lesson will unpack the author's argument and its theological significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author first presents the objection (that Ali delayed) and then gives a concise initial response: the pledge did happen, so consensus is valid.
The author does not deny the delay but uses it to build a stronger case.
2
Understand the explanation for Ali's delay
The author explains that Ali's delay was due to careful reflection to determine the truth. Consider why this explanation is important—it shows that Ali was not opposing but seeking certainty.
Ali's hesitation was a virtue, not a flaw.
3
Examine the refutation of the taqiyyah claim
The author refutes the Rawafid claim that Ali acted out of fear (taqiyyah) by pointing to Ali's brave character. Note how the author uses Ali's known qualities to argue against the claim.
The author finds it contradictory to claim Ali was both courageous and acting out of fear.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how the author uses both positive reasoning (Ali's reflection) and negative refutation (character argument) to defend the consensus. This is a typical kalam method.
The goal is to show that the objection does not harm the Sunni position.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: دعوى الإجماع ممنوعة فإن عليًا تأخر عن بيعته ستة أشهر أو أربعة أشهر. قلنا: وقد انعقد بعد بيعته وبه يحتج فلم يكن في هذا السؤال لهم منفعة، ثم تأخره محمول على الاشتغال بالنظر ليتضح له وجوه الصواب فيتابع عامة الصحابة ويبايعه، أو يبدو له وجه خطئهم فيعلن مخالفتهم، ويجاهر بالمكاشفة ويشهر عليهم سيفه، كما يليق بكمال علمه وقوة ديانته ورباطة جأشه ، ولذا لم يظهر في تلك المدة والمنازعة، فلما لاح له بعد طول التردي وإدمان النظر وجه الصواب وافق غيره من الصحابة وبايع الصديق اتباعًا للحق كما هو اللائق بحاله في جلالة قدره وعظم شأنه، لا خوفا على نفسه وأهاليه وتوقيًا على مكروه يناله في نفسه كما بهم الروافض، إذ هو بعيد عن حاله ممتنع منه على ما تقدم من ذكره في بيان شهامته وصرامته ومشهور نجدته وشجاعته ومخبور غنائه وبسالته، وما عرف من صلابته في ديانته وقوة عشيرته وعزة قبيلته. ومن أعجب الأشياء دعوى الروافض أن عليًا بايع أبا بكر تقية مع ما يدعون من وصف أبي بكر بالضعف والجبن ووصف علي بالقوة وغاية الشجاعة والله الموفق.
For they said: The claim of consensus is denied because Ali delayed his pledge of allegiance by six months or four months. We said: It was concluded after his pledge and it is used as evidence, so there is no benefit to them in this question. Then his delay is attributed to preoccupation with reflection so that the aspects of correctness become clear to him, so he follows the generality of the Companions and pledges allegiance, or the aspect of their error becomes apparent to him so he declares his opposition, and he openly discloses and draws his sword against them, as befits the perfection of his knowledge, the strength of his religiosity, and his firmness of heart. Therefore, during that period, no dispute appeared. When, after prolonged hesitation and continuous reflection, the aspect of correctness became clear to him, he agreed with the other Companions and pledged allegiance to al-Siddiq (Abu Bakr) following the truth, as is fitting for his high rank and great standing, not out of fear for himself and his family, nor out of caution against harm that might befall him as the Rawafid claim, for he is far from that state and it is impossible for him, based on what has preceded in mentioning his bravery and resolution, his known valor and courage, his well-known sufficiency and prowess, and what is known of his firmness in his religion, the strength of his clan, and the honor of his tribe. And among the most amazing things is the claim of the Rawafid that Ali pledged allegiance to Abu Bakr out of taqiyyah (dissimulation), despite what they claim of describing Abu Bakr as weak and cowardly, and describing Ali as strong and extremely courageous. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 1138

Commentary
This is an objection-and-response passage. The objection claims that consensus on Abu Bakr's caliphate is not proven because Ali delayed his pledge by several months. The author responds by affirming that the pledge did eventually occur, so consensus is valid. He explains that Ali's delay was due to careful deliberation to determine the truth, and once he was convinced, he joined the majority. The author then refutes the Rawafid claim that Ali acted out of taqiyyah (dissimulation), arguing that such an act is incompatible with Ali's well-known bravery, strength of faith, and noble character. The passage closes by highlighting the absurdity of the Rawafid position, given that they also portray Ali as courageous while claiming he acted out of fear.

Key Terms
بيعة
تقية
إجماع
روافض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ali's delayed pledge shows he opposed Abu Bakr's caliphate and consensus was not achieved.
Correction: Ali's delay was for reflection; he eventually pledged, so consensus was valid, and his character precludes taqiyyah.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that Ali's delayed bay'ah did not invalidate consensus?
A) Because Ali eventually pledged and his delay was for reflection.
B) Because Ali's pledge was not required for consensus.
C) Because the other Companions forced him to pledge.
D) Because the pledge was invalid anyway.

Key Takeaway
Ali's delayed bay'ah did not invalidate consensus because it was motivated by deliberate reflection, and the claim of taqiyyah is inconsistent with his known courage.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a decision that tests your commitment to truth, do you honestly examine your motives, or do you let fear of what others think guide you? What would change if you followed Ali's example of prolonged reflection and then acting on the truth without regard for worldly consequences?
?
The author insists that the claim of taqiyyah is incompatible with Ali's known courage. Have you ever made excuses for compromising your faith or principles, claiming necessity or fear? What is one area of your life where you need to replace caution with courageous integrity?
?
Ali's delay was a season of intense personal deliberation about the right path. When was the last time you set aside significant time to seek clarity on a matter of truth, without rushing to a conclusion? Will you commit to doing that now for a pressing spiritual decision?

The Impossibility of Hidden Dissent in Consensus
Refutes the possibility of hidden dissent in consensus on Abu Bakr's caliphate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that any possible dissent against Abu Bakr's caliphate would have become publicly known, and that his own statement is sufficient proof for the validity of consensus.

Core Idea
If someone truly dissented, it would have become famous.

The author responds to an objection that consensus on Abu Bakr's caliphate is invalid because some might have dissented secretly. He first states that Abu Bakr's own acceptance is a proof due to mass transmission. Then he argues that any real dissent would have been impossible to conceal given the nature of such a significant event. This establishes the reliability of consensus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection being refuted
Read the first few lines to understand the claim that consensus is invalid if some dissented.
The objector imagines that someone might have dissented without it being known.
2
Notice the first argument: Abu Bakr's statement is sufficient proof
The author argues that Abu Bakr's own pledge and acceptance of caliphate, transmitted by tawatur, is a sufficient proof.
This shows that consensus is not even needed for validity.
3
Understand the second argument: dissent would have been known
The author argues that if there was any real dissent, it would have become widely known because such a significant event cannot be concealed.
This is based on normal custom and the nature of the Companions.
4
Consider the implication
The author warns that if hidden dissent were allowed, one could also deny the transmission of the Quran and miracles.
This shows the dangerous consequences of the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن رأي عليه وقوله عندهم حجة، وقد ثبت بالنقل المتواتر الذي ينسب جاحده إلى العناد والمكابرة بيعته أبا بكر واعترافه بخلافته، فيكون قوله حجة كافية لصحة خلافته، ولا يقع لهم فيما يزعمون أن الإجماع مع مخالفة البعض غير منعقد ويوهم أن بعضا خالف ولم يعلم يقينا عدم مخالفة البعض لأن خلاف البعض لو توهم فعلی به ثبت وفاقه وقوله عندكم حجة كافية، ثم نقول لو وجد خلاف البعض لظهر إذ لا يتوهم في العادات الجارية خلاف من يعد بخلافه خلافا في مثل هذه الحادثة ثم تتكتم ولا تشتهر، ولو جاز ذا لجاز أن يزعم ملحد أن القرآن لعله عورض ولم ينقل معارضة، وكذا معجزة كل نبي فيصير ذلك طريقًا لإبطال الرسالات وتعطيل المعجزات والآيات التي أتى بها الرسل عليهم السلام، وذا كفر وباطل فكذا ما أدى إليه، بل فيه إنكار الشاهدات وجحد الضروريات وإفساد الألسنة واللغات، إذ ما من كلام يتكلم به إلا ويمكن لقائل أن يقول إن ما ذكرت من الألفاظ ليست بدلالة على ما قصدت إليه من المعاني إذ لم يثبت ذلك باتفاق أهل اللسان أو مساعدة أهل البلدة أو القبيلة إذ يتوهم أن بعضهم يجعل هذه الألفاظ عبارات عن أضداد هذه المعاني، وما هذا سبيله لا يجوز التفات إليه ولا الاشتغال بجواب من جعله معلولاً عليه لتأييد نحلته وتقوية رأيه ومذهبه.
As for the fact that his opinion and statement are an authoritative proof for them, and it has been established through mass transmission (tawatur) that the denier of which is attributed to obstinacy and stubbornness, that Abu Bakr paid allegiance and acknowledged his caliphate, thus his statement is a sufficient proof for the validity of his caliphate. And what they claim does not occur to them, that consensus with the opposition of some is not formed, and it is imagined that some opposed while it is not known with certainty that there was no opposition from some, because if opposition from some is imagined, then his agreement and statement are established as a sufficient proof for you. Then we say: if opposition from some existed, it would have appeared, for it is not imagined in normal custom that opposition from one whose opposition is counted as opposition in such an event would then be concealed and not become famous. And if this were allowed, then an atheist could claim that the Quran might have been contested but the contestation was not transmitted, and similarly for the miracle of every prophet, thus making it a way to invalidate prophethood and nullify the miracles and signs brought by the messengers, peace be upon them. And that is disbelief and falsehood, and likewise what leads to it. Rather, it entails denial of witnessed facts, rejection of necessities, and corruption of tongues and languages, for there is no speech spoken but a speaker could say: 'What you mentioned of words are not indicative of the meanings you intended, since that is not established by the agreement of the people of the language or the support of the people of the town or tribe, because it is imagined that some of them make these words expressions for the opposites of these meanings.' And whatever is on this way, it is not permissible to pay attention to it nor to occupy oneself with answering one who makes it a flaw to support his sect and strengthen his opinion and school.
Source pages p. 1139

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection that consensus on Abu Bakr's caliphate is invalid because some might have dissented. The author begins by stating that Abu Bakr's own statement accepting the caliphate is itself a proof for its validity, given the mass transmission of his pledge. He then argues that even if one imagines dissent, the dissenter's agreement would be established by their actions. He continues that if dissent existed it would have become publicly known, since in normal circumstances such a significant event cannot be hidden. He draws a parallel: if one could claim that dissent was concealed, then an atheist could similarly claim that the Quran was contested without the contestation being reported, or that prophets' miracles were challenged secretly. This would undermine all prophethood, which is disbelief. Furthermore, such reasoning would destroy the reliability of language itself, because one could always say that words might mean their opposites. Therefore, such an argument is invalid and does not deserve attention.

Key Terms
إجماع
تواتر
حجة
خلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Consensus is invalid if any individual secretly dissented, even if that dissent is unknown.
Correction: The author argues that if dissent existed, it would have become known due to the significance of the event, and therefore undisclosed dissent does not affect the validity of consensus.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what would happen if there was any real dissent against Abu Bakr's caliphate?
A) It would remain hidden forever.
B) It would have become publicly known.
C) It would invalidate the caliphate.
D) It would be irrelevant because of Abu Bakr's statement.

Key Takeaway
Hidden dissent against a well-known consensus is impossible because such dissent would have become publicly known.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a well-established truth in your heart because you imagined there might be secret objections you haven't heard? What does this passage teach you about the strength of widespread testimony?
?
If you allow yourself to doubt what has been mass-transmitted simply because you imagine unknown opposition, what else could you doubt? Are you willing to face that consequence?
?
This argument shows that hidden dissent against a known consensus is impossible. Think of a truth you hold because of consensus among believers. Do you actually trust that consensus, or do you harbor secret doubts that you haven't confronted?

The Consequences of Denying Reliable Transmission
Shows that accepting hidden dissent leads to denial of all forms of reliable transmission, including language.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how allowing the possibility of hidden dissent logically leads to the denial of all linguistic and religious transmission, making language itself unreliable.

Core Idea
If hidden dissent is accepted, no speech or religious truth can be reliably known.

The author extends his argument to show that the same reasoning used to doubt consensus could be used to deny the reliability of language itself. If one can claim that someone may have dissented without being known, then one can also claim that words might mean their opposites. This would destroy all communication and religion.

Guided Path
1
Notice the claim: denial of witnessed facts and corruption of languages
Read the opening line where the author states that the objection leads to denying witnessed facts and corrupting tongues and languages.
This is the first consequence he lists.
2
Understand the example: words might mean the opposite
The author gives an example: someone could always say that words might mean the opposite of what is intended, because there is no absolute proof that all language users agree.
This is a hypothetical that shows the absurdity of the objection.
3
Realize the invalidity of such arguments
The author concludes that such a line of reasoning should not be given any attention or response, as it is merely a way to support one's sect.
This shows that the objection is not worthy of serious consideration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن رأي عليه وقوله عندهم حجة، وقد ثبت بالنقل المتواتر الذي ينسب جاحده إلى العناد والمكابرة بيعته أبا بكر واعترافه بخلافته، فيكون قوله حجة كافية لصحة خلافته، ولا يقع لهم فيما يزعمون أن الإجماع مع مخالفة البعض غير منعقد ويوهم أن بعضا خالف ولم يعلم يقينا عدم مخالفة البعض لأن خلاف البعض لو توهم فعلی به ثبت وفاقه وقوله عندكم حجة كافية، ثم نقول لو وجد خلاف البعض لظهر إذ لا يتوهم في العادات الجارية خلاف من يعد بخلافه خلافا في مثل هذه الحادثة ثم تتكتم ولا تشتهر، ولو جاز ذا لجاز أن يزعم ملحد أن القرآن لعله عورض ولم ينقل معارضة، وكذا معجزة كل نبي فيصير ذلك طريقًا لإبطال الرسالات وتعطيل المعجزات والآيات التي أتى بها الرسل عليهم السلام، وذا كفر وباطل فكذا ما أدى إليه، بل فيه إنكار الشاهدات وجحد الضروريات وإفساد الألسنة واللغات، إذ ما من كلام يتكلم به إلا ويمكن لقائل أن يقول إن ما ذكرت من الألفاظ ليست بدلالة على ما قصدت إليه من المعاني إذ لم يثبت ذلك باتفاق أهل اللسان أو مساعدة أهل البلدة أو القبيلة إذ يتوهم أن بعضهم يجعل هذه الألفاظ عبارات عن أضداد هذه المعاني، وما هذا سبيله لا يجوز التفات إليه ولا الاشتغال بجواب من جعله معلولاً عليه لتأييد نحلته وتقوية رأيه ومذهبه.
As for the fact that his opinion and statement are an authoritative proof for them, and it has been established through mass transmission (tawatur) that the denier of which is attributed to obstinacy and stubbornness, that Abu Bakr paid allegiance and acknowledged his caliphate, thus his statement is a sufficient proof for the validity of his caliphate. And what they claim does not occur to them, that consensus with the opposition of some is not formed, and it is imagined that some opposed while it is not known with certainty that there was no opposition from some, because if opposition from some is imagined, then his agreement and statement are established as a sufficient proof for you. Then we say: if opposition from some existed, it would have appeared, for it is not imagined in normal custom that opposition from one whose opposition is counted as opposition in such an event would then be concealed and not become famous. And if this were allowed, then an atheist could claim that the Quran might have been contested but the contestation was not transmitted, and similarly for the miracle of every prophet, thus making it a way to invalidate prophethood and nullify the miracles and signs brought by the messengers, peace be upon them. And that is disbelief and falsehood, and likewise what leads to it. Rather, it entails denial of witnessed facts, rejection of necessities, and corruption of tongues and languages, for there is no speech spoken but a speaker could say: 'What you mentioned of words are not indicative of the meanings you intended, since that is not established by the agreement of the people of the language or the support of the people of the town or tribe, because it is imagined that some of them make these words expressions for the opposites of these meanings.' And whatever is on this way, it is not permissible to pay attention to it nor to occupy oneself with answering one who makes it a flaw to support his sect and strengthen his opinion and school.
Source pages p. 1139

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection that consensus on Abu Bakr's caliphate is invalid because some might have dissented. The author begins by stating that Abu Bakr's own statement accepting the caliphate is itself a proof for its validity, given the mass transmission of his pledge. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Doubting consensus is a harmless academic exercise.
Correction: The author shows that such doubting leads to the destruction of the foundations of language and religion, and is therefore not a trivial matter.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is one consequence of allowing the possibility of hidden dissent?
A) It strengthens the authority of scholars.
B) It makes language unreliable because words could mean their opposites.
C) It proves the caliphate was invalid.
D) It is a sign of strong faith.

Key Takeaway
Accepting hidden dissent logically leads to the destruction of reliable linguistic and religious transmission.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear someone claim that the Quran might have been changed or that its meaning is uncertain because of differing interpretations, do you feel your conviction waver? How does this passage call you to reject such doubts as fundamentally destructive to all knowledge?
?
Consider a time you accepted a rumor or weak report about someone’s character. How did that affect your trust in them? In light of this lesson, how does unreliable transmission erode not just religious knowledge, but all human relationships and communication?
?
Are there any areas in your life where you rely on weak evidence or allow 'hidden dissent' to create doubt about clear truths? What would change if you applied the same standard of reliability to your faith as you do to everyday speech?
